Relative vs Absolute Impossibility
Ghazali poses a question about the nature of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between relative impossibility (relative to human power) and absolute impossibility (impossible in itself), and why this distinction is crucial for the debate about divine power.

Core Idea
Not every human incapacity is a sign of absolute impossibility.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the assumption that what is impossible for humans must be impossible for God. He asks a fundamental question: is the impossibility of creating from nothing relative to human capacity, or is it absolute? This sets the stage for a deeper analysis of divine power and logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The dispute is about whether creation from nothing is possible for God, based on human experience of inability.
Ghazali is responding to philosophers who deny creation ex nihilo.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The impossibility of bringing something from nothing could be relative (only for humans) or absolute (in itself).
3
Analyze the implications of each
If relative, the argument shifts to human limitation; if absolute, one must justify a general metaphysical claim from limited experience.
This leads to the question of valid inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن يكن الثانى ، وهو أن الأمر مرده إلى التجربة ، وأنا قد حاولنا إيجاد شيء من لا شي . شيء ، فلم نستطع . فهل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لا شيء وعدم إمكان صيرورة الشيء إلى لا شيء ، قضية نسبية ؟ أم قضية مطلقة ؟ أعنى أنه هل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لاشيء ، وعدم إمكان تصيير الشيء إلى لا شيء تعنى أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ؟ أم أنه غير ممكن فقط بالنسبة إلى قدرة البشر ؟ فإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد بيان عدم إمكان ذلك بالنسبة لقدرة البشر . فهو تحول عن أصل موضوع النزاع وإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ، فكيف يستدل على حكم عام بدليل غير عام ؟ وهل يسوغ الانتقال إلى الحكم بعدم قدرة الخالق على ذلك ، من عجز قدرة المخلوق عليه ؟ نعم إن القدرتين قد يتساويان بالنسبة لبعض الأمور ، كالمستحيلات الذاتية ، مثل الجمع بين النقيضين ، أو رفعهما ، فهذه مما لا تتعلق بها قدرة الخالق ، ولا تدركها قدرة المخلوق ؛ ولكن ليس كلما عجزت قدرة المخلوق ، لم تتعلق قدرة الخالق ؛ لهذا يسوغ الإصغاء إلى من يتخذ من عجز البشر عن إيجاد شيء من لا شي دليلا على استحالة الأمر في ذاته
And if it is the second, namely that the matter reduces to experience, and we have tried to bring a thing from nothing, but we could not. So is the proposition of the impossibility of bringing a thing from nothing and the impossibility of a thing becoming nothing a relative proposition or an absolute proposition? I mean, does the proposition of the impossibility of bringing a thing from nothing and the impossibility of making a thing become nothing mean that it is impossible in itself? Or that it is impossible only in relation to human power? If it is the second, namely that the intended meaning is to show the impossibility of that in relation to human power, then it is a shift from the original subject of dispute. And if it is the second, namely that the intended meaning is that it is impossible in itself, then how can a general judgment be inferred from a non-general proof? And is it permissible to transition to the judgment of the Creator's inability to do that from the inability of the creature's power over it? Yes, the two powers may be equal with respect to some matters, like essential impossibilities (al-mustahīlāt al-dhātiyyah), such as combining two opposites or removing them; these are such that the Creator's power does not pertain to them, nor does the creature's power grasp them. But not every time the creature's power is incapable does the Creator's power not pertain; for this reason it is permissible to listen to one who takes the inability of humans to bring a thing from nothing as evidence for the impossibility of the matter in itself.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the meaning of 'impossibility' in the context of creation from nothing. Ghazali presents a dichotomy: either the impossibility is relative (nishī) i.e., only relative to human power, or absolute (muṭlaq) i.e., impossible in itself. If it is relative, then the argument shifts from the original dispute about divine power to a trivial point about human limitation. If it is absolute, then one must justify inferring a general metaphysical impossibility from a limited human experience. Ghazali concedes that for some things, like contradictions (e.g., combining two opposites), human and divine power are equal in their inability. However, he warns that not every human incapacity implies divine incapacity. Therefore, one cannot simply argue from human inability to absolute impossibility, though some try. This guards against overgeneralizing from human limitation to divine limitation.

Key Terms
قضية نسبية
قضية مطلقة
قدرة البشر
قدرة الخالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans cannot do something, then no being can do it.
Correction: Ghazali shows this confuses relative and absolute impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first logical possibility regarding the impossibility of creating from nothing?
A) It is absolutely impossible in itself.
B) It is impossible only relative to human power.
C) It is impossible only relative to divine power.
D) It is not impossible at all.

Key Takeaway
The burden lies on proving that impossibility is absolute, not merely relative to human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually assumed that because something is impossible for you, it must be impossible in reality? Where in your life do you habitually confuse your own limits with absolute limits? How does this lesson force you to rethink that default assumption?
?
When you face a deep limitation—financially, intellectually, or spiritually—do you tend to attribute it to the nature of the universe or to your own weakness? What would change in your daily outlook if you truly internalized the distinction between relative and absolute impossibility?
?
What does the ease with which you slide from 'I cannot do this' to 'This cannot happen' reveal about your understanding of God's power versus your own? Will you now catch yourself making that leap, and let the lesson pierce your heart?

Essential Impossibilities and Divine Power
Ghazali discusses essential impossibilities and the limits of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's concept of essential impossibilities (like contradictions) and how divine power relates to them, as well as the argument against inferring divine impotence from human impotence.

Core Idea
God's power is limited only by logical contradictions, not by human limitations.

After establishing the distinction, Ghazali clarifies that in some cases, such as combining opposites, divine and human power are equally incapable. But he warns against generalizing human incapacity to God. This lesson examines the category of essential impossibilities and the logical fallacy of overgeneralization.

Guided Path
1
Recognize essential impossibilities
Essential impossibilities are things like combining two opposites or removing both. These are impossible in themselves.
These are logical contradictions.
2
Understand the equality of powers
In these cases, both divine and human power are incapable. Ghazali concedes this point.
3
Grasp the non-equality
For other matters, human incapacity does not entail divine incapacity. Divine power is greater.
This is the key corrective.
4
The conclusion
Therefore, one cannot take human inability as proof of absolute impossibility.
Ghazali allows listening to such arguments only as a starting point, not as a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن يكن الثانى ، وهو أن الأمر مرده إلى التجربة ، وأنا قد حاولنا إيجاد شيء من لا شي . شيء ، فلم نستطع . فهل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لا شيء وعدم إمكان صيرورة الشيء إلى لا شيء ، قضية نسبية ؟ أم قضية مطلقة ؟ أعنى أنه هل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لاشيء ، وعدم إمكان تصيير الشيء إلى لا شيء تعنى أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ؟ أم أنه غير ممكن فقط بالنسبة إلى قدرة البشر ؟ فإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد بيان عدم إمكان ذلك بالنسبة لقدرة البشر . فهو تحول عن أصل موضوع النزاع وإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ، فكيف يستدل على حكم عام بدليل غير عام ؟ وهل يسوغ الانتقال إلى الحكم بعدم قدرة الخالق على ذلك ، من عجز قدرة المخلوق عليه ؟ نعم إن القدرتين قد يتساويان بالنسبة لبعض الأمور ، كالمستحيلات الذاتية ، مثل الجمع بين النقيضين ، أو رفعهما ، فهذه مما لا تتعلق بها قدرة الخالق ، ولا تدركها قدرة المخلوق ؛ ولكن ليس كلما عجزت قدرة المخلوق ، لم تتعلق قدرة الخالق ؛ لهذا يسوغ الإصغاء إلى من يتخذ من عجز البشر عن إيجاد شيء من لا شي دليلا على استحالة الأمر في ذاته
And if it is the second, namely that the matter reduces to experience, and we have tried to bring a thing from nothing, but we could not. So is the proposition of the impossibility of bringing a thing from nothing and the impossibility of a thing becoming nothing a relative proposition or an absolute proposition? I mean, does the proposition of the impossibility of bringing a thing from nothing and the impossibility of making a thing become nothing mean that it is impossible in itself? Or that it is impossible only in relation to human power? If it is the second, namely that the intended meaning is to show the impossibility of that in relation to human power, then it is a shift from the original subject of dispute. And if it is the second, namely that the intended meaning is that it is impossible in itself, then how can a general judgment be inferred from a non-general proof? And is it permissible to transition to the judgment of the Creator's inability to do that from the inability of the creature's power over it? Yes, the two powers may be equal with respect to some matters, like essential impossibilities (al-mustahīlāt al-dhātiyyah), such as combining two opposites or removing them; these are such that the Creator's power does not pertain to them, nor does the creature's power grasp them. But not every time the creature's power is incapable does the Creator's power not pertain; for this reason it is permissible to listen to one who takes the inability of humans to bring a thing from nothing as evidence for the impossibility of the matter in itself.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the meaning of 'impossibility' in the context of creation from nothing. Ghazali presents a dichotomy: either the impossibility is relative (nishī) i.e., only relative to human power, or absolute (muṭlaq) i.e., impossible in itself. If it is relative, then the argument shifts from the original dispute about divine power to a trivial point about human limitation. If it is absolute, then one must justify inferring a general metaphysical impossibility from a limited human experience. However, he warns that not every human incapacity implies divine incapacity. Therefore, one cannot simply argue from human inability to absolute impossibility, though some try. This guards against overgeneralizing from human limitation to divine limitation.

Key Terms

الجمع بين النقيضين
قدرة الخالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is limited by everything humans cannot do.
Correction: God's power is only limited by essential impossibilities, not human experiences.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, in which matters are divine and human power equal?
A) All matters of creation from nothing.
B) Essential impossibilities like contradictions.
C) Matters of experience and observation.
D) No matters at all.

Key Takeaway
Essential impossibilities (like contradictions) are the only things that limit divine power, not human experiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you once said, 'That's impossible, not even God can do that.' What was the true reason? Were you confusing your limited perspective with a logical impossibility?
?
If only logical contradictions are impossible for God, what does that mean for a prayer you have given up on because it seemed too hard? Will you pray it again today, with full trust?
?
When you feel helpless and incapable, do you tend to project your limitations onto God? How can you remind yourself that His power is not bound by your weakness?

Ghazali Refutes the Philosophers' Appeal to Necessity by Pointing to Their Own Contradictory Doctrines
Ghazali counters by exposing inconsistency in the philosophers' own beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a dialectical method to turn the philosophers' appeal to necessity against them by showing that their own doctrine of divine knowledge appears impossible by the same standard.

Core Idea
A double standard is no argument: the philosophers must apply necessity consistently.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to the philosophers' claim that a complete cause must have an effect by necessity. Rather than directly denying this, he points out that the philosophers themselves hold a belief—that God knows all things with a single, simple knowledge—which appears rationally impossible from our perspective. This forces the philosophers either to abandon their appeal to necessity or to accept that their own theology is irrational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal English translation. Let the dialectical structure come through before analyzing it.
Notice the objection-counter structure.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers assert that it is known by necessity that a complete cause cannot be without its effect. Find this statement and understand its role.
This is their appeal to necessity.
3
See Ghazali's counter
Ghazali retorts by saying the philosophers themselves believe in a seemingly impossible doctrine: that God's knowledge is one, simple, and not multiple despite multiple objects. Locate this counter.
He is using their own standard against them.
4
Understand the extreme response
Some philosophers, sensing the difficulty, claimed God knows only Himself with identity of knower, knowledge, and known. See how Ghazali uses this to further show inconsistency.
This leads to the absurdity that God would not know His own creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل نحن بضرورة العقل نعلم أنه لا يتصور موجيب بتمام شروطه من غير موجب ، ومجوز ذلك مكابر لضرورة العقل. قلنا : وما الفصل بينكم وبين خصومكم ، إذا قالوا لكم : إنا بالضرورة نعلم إحالة قول من يقول : إن ذاتاً واحدة عالمة بجميع الكليات من غير أن يوجب ذلك كثرة ، ومن غير أن يكون العلم زيادة على الذات ، ومن غير أن يتعدد العلم مع تعدد المعلوم ، وهذا مذهبكم فى حق الله ، وهو بالنسبة إلينا وإلى علومنا في غاية الإحالة ، ولكن تقولون ؛ لا يقاس العلم القديم بالحادث وطائفة منكم استشعروا إحالة هذا فقالوا : إن الله لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فهو العاقل ، وهو العقل ، وهو المعقول ؛ والكل واحد - فلو قال قائل : اتحاد العاقل ، والعقل ، والمعقول ، معلوم الاستحالة بالضرورة ؛ إذ تقدير صانع للعالم لا يعلم صنعته ، محال بالضرورة ، والقديم إذا لم يعلم إلا نفسه – تعالى عن قولكم ، وعن قول جميع الزائغين علوا كبيراً – – لم يكن يعلم صنعته ألبتة
For if it is said: We know by necessity of reason that it is inconceivable for a causer (mūjib) with all its conditions to be without an effect (mūjab), and one who allows that is contending against the necessity of reason. We say: What is the distinction between you and your opponents when they say to you: We know by necessity the impossibility of the statement that one essence knows all universals without that entailing multiplicity, and without knowledge being an addition to the essence, and without knowledge being multiple with the multiplicity of the known — and this is your doctrine concerning God, and it is in the utmost impossibility relative to us and our sciences. But you say: The eternal knowledge is not to be compared with temporal knowledge, and a group among you, sensing the impossibility of this, said: God knows only Himself, so He is the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible; all are one. So if someone said: The union of the intellect, intelligent, and intelligible is known by necessity to be impossible, because it is impossible by necessity that the Maker of the world does not know His handiwork, and if the Eternal knows only Himself — exalted is He above your saying and above the saying of all deviators in great exaltation — then He would not know His handiwork at all.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and Ghazālī's counter. The philosophers insist that it is known by necessity that a complete cause cannot be without its effect. Ghazālī responds by turning the tables. He points out that the philosophers themselves hold a doctrine that appears impossible by necessity: namely, that God knows all particulars with a single, simple knowledge that does not cause multiplicity or add to His essence. From a human perspective, this is impossible. But the philosophers argue that divine knowledge is incomparable. Some among them even went so far as to say God knows only Himself, equating knower, knowledge, and known. Ghazālī then says: if someone were to claim that the union of knower, knowledge, and known is impossible by necessity, and that God must know His creation, then the philosophers would be inconsistent. He highlights the double standard: the philosophers demand necessity for their own principles but reject it when the same standard is applied to their own doctrines.

Key Terms
العاقل والعقل والمعقول
علم قديم
كليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazālī is denying that God's knowledge is incomparable.
Correction: Ghazālī is not denying incomparability; he is using the philosophers' own doctrine to show their inconsistency: they accept what seems impossible by necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazālī bring up the philosophers' doctrine of God's knowledge?
A) To prove that God knows all things.
B) To show that they themselves accept things that seem impossible by necessity.
C) To argue that knowledge must be multiple.
D) To reject all rational arguments.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' appeal to necessity is inconsistent because they themselves accept a doctrine of divine knowledge that appears impossible by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you held others to a standard of consistency that you fail to apply to your own beliefs or actions? How does that double standard affect your relationship with Allah's commands?
?
The philosophers accepted a doctrine that seemed impossible to reason while rejecting another on the same basis. Where in your life do you make exceptions for your own desires while claiming to follow divine guidance? Be specific and commit to one change.
?
Are you willing to accept a truth from revelation that appears contrary to your human reasoning, or do you, like the philosophers, demand that everything fit your intellectual framework? What is one area where you need to submit your reason to Allah's word?

Thomas Aquinas on Creation: A Middle Ground
Editor quotes Yusuf Karam summarizing Aquinas's creation theory and refutation of emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Thomas Aquinas refutes both emanation and pantheism while affirming creation as the exclusive act of God.

Core Idea
Aquinas argues creation requires infinite power, so only God can create from nothing.

This passage introduces Thomas Aquinas's theory of creation as a middle ground between Islamic philosophers and theologians. It presents three key arguments: that existence by participation is not emanation, that the 'from one only one' maxim applies only to natural agents, and that creation requires infinite power. These points help clarify why only God can create.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the overall structure: an introduction followed by three numbered arguments.
The passage is an editorial summary, not Aquinas's own words.
2
Understand the first argument: existence by participation
Aquinas argues that everything except God exists by participation, not by emanation. Emanation would make the world like God, which it is not. This also refutes pantheism.
Key Arabic terms: 'الوجود القائم بذاته' and 'المشاركة'.
3
Analyze the critique of Ibn Sina's maxim
The 'from one only one' rule applies to natural agents, not voluntary agents. Since God knows many things, He can create many things directly.
Focus on 'الفاعل الإرادي' (voluntary agent) versus natural agent.
4
Explore why creatures cannot create
A creature does not exist by itself and cannot grant existence. The gap from non-existence to existence is infinite, so only God with infinite power can create.
This is the final and strongest point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعل من الطريف أن أسوق هذا رأى فيلسوف مسيحى – كان همه أيضاً أن يوفق بين الدين والفلسفة - عاش فى العصور الوسطى كما عاش ابن سينا والغزالى ، ويبدو اطلع على وجهة نظرهما ، فهداه تشددهما إلى إمكان وجود حل وسط ، ذلكم هو القديس توما الأكويني الذي عاش بين سنتی ١٢٢٥ ، ١٢٧٤ م ، والذي يصور الأستاذ يوسف كرم نظرية الخلق عنده على الوجه الآتى : (۱) كل موجود - ما خلا الله - مخلوق من الله ضرورة ؛ لأن الوجود القائم بذاته لا يمكن أن يكون إلا واحداً ؛ فيلزم أن كل ما خلا الله ليس عين وجوده ، ولكنه موجود بالمشاركة وليس الوجود بالمشاركة صدوراً عن ذات الله ، كما تقول الأفلاطونية الحديثة ؛ لأن ما يصدر عن الذات صدوراً ضرورياً ، فهو مثل الذات ، وليس العالم مثل الله بالطبع . هذه الناحية أيضاً يسقط مذهب وحدة الوجود ، الذي يعتبر العالم مظهراً الله ومن . أما قول ابن سينا : إن من شأن الواحد دائماً أن يصدر عنه واحد ، فيصدق على الفاعل ، لا على الفاعل الإرادى الذى يفعل بالصورة المعقولة ، ولما كان الله يتعقل أموراً كثيرة ، فهو يقدر أن يفعل أشياء كثيرة . يضاف إلى ذلك استحالة صدور الموجودات بعضها عن بعض ؛ لأن المخلوق غير موجود بذاته ، فلا يستطيع أن يمنح وجوداً ليس له بالذات ، ولئن كان المخلوق الموجود متناهياً ، فإن المسافة بين اللاوجود والوجود لا متناهية ، فالخلق يقتضى قدرة لا متناهية ؛ لذلك كان خاصاً بالله وحده
It is perhaps interesting for me to present the view of a Christian philosopher—whose concern was also to reconcile religion and philosophy—who lived in the Middle Ages as Ibn Sina and Ghazali lived, and it seems he was aware of their perspectives, and their rigidity guided him to the possibility of a middle ground. That is St. Thomas Aquinas, who lived between 1225 and 1274 CE, and whose theory of creation Professor Yusuf Karam describes as follows: (1) Every existent—except God—is necessarily created by God, because self-subsistent existence cannot be but one; thus it follows that everything except God is not identical with His existence, but exists by participation, and existence by participation is not emanation from God's essence, as the Neo-Platonists say, because what emanates from the essence by necessary emanation is like the essence, and the world is not like God of course. This aspect also refutes pantheism, which considers the world a manifestation of God and [؟]. As for Ibn Sina's statement that it is the property of the one to always produce one, it applies to the agent, not to the voluntary agent who acts through an intelligible form. Since God apprehends many things, He is able to do many things. Added to that is the impossibility of the emanation of existents one from another, because the creature does not exist by itself, so it cannot grant an existence that it does not possess by essence. Even if the existing creature is finite, the distance between non-existence and existence is infinite, so creation requires infinite power; therefore it was exclusive to God alone.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction to Thomas Aquinas's theory of creation, presented as a middle ground between the philosophers and theologians. The editor notes that Aquinas, like Ibn Sina and Ghazali, sought to reconcile religion and philosophy, and his view is summarized from Yusuf Karam. The first point states that everything except God is necessarily created by God, because self-subsistent existence is unique to God. Other existents exist by participation, not by emanation from God's essence as Neo-Platonists claim, because emanation would produce something like the essence, but the world is not like God. This also refutes pantheism. The second point addresses Ibn Sina's 'from one only one proceeds' maxim: it applies to natural agents, not to a voluntary agent who acts by intelligible form. God apprehends many things, so He can do many things. Finally, it argues against emanation of existents from each other because a creature does not exist by itself and cannot grant existence. Since the finite creature's distance from non-existence is infinite, creation requires infinite power, hence exclusive to God.

Key Terms
الخلق
الصدور
الوجود القائم بذاته
المشاركة
الفاعل الإرادي
وحدة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Aquinas's view is essentially the same as emanationism.
Correction: Aquinas explicitly rejects emanation because it would produce something like God, but the world is not like God. He affirms creation as a voluntary act requiring infinite power.

Checkpoint
According to Aquinas as presented here, why does existence by participation not involve emanation from God's essence?
A) Because emanation would produce something like God, but the world is not like God.
B) Because existence by participation is the same as emanation.
C) Because God does not will the world.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Creation requires infinite power and a voluntary agent, so only God can create from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your very existence is a gift created from nothing, or do you live as if you have some inherent right to exist? How would your daily attitude change if you consistently remembered that you exist only by God's infinite power?
?
Aquinas argues that a creature cannot grant existence because it does not possess existence by itself. In what areas of your life do you wrongly depend on finite things—people, status, achievements—as if they could give you lasting existence or worth?
?
This passage presents creation as an act of a voluntary agent with intelligence and will. When you think of God as your Creator, do you relate to Him as a personal, purposeful Agent, or as an impersonal force? How might that distinction affect your prayer and trust in Him?

Divine Choice and the Resolution of the Eternity Debate
Editor explains Aquinas's resolution of the eternity debate through divine will and limits of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of divine choice (al-ikhtiyar) resolves the debate on the world's eternity versus temporality, and why rational arguments alone are insufficient.

Core Idea
Divine choice, not rational proof, resolves the eternity debate.

This passage presents a key insight from Aquinas (as edited by Yusuf Karam) that shifts the debate from rational demonstration to divine revelation. It explains that God's necessary will is only for His essence, while His will for creation is free choice, making both eternity and temporality rational possibilities that can only be settled by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the key terms and the logical flow of the argument.
Pay special attention to the distinction between necessary will and choice.
2
Identify the two types of divine will
The passage distinguishes between God's necessary will (only for Himself) and His will by choice (for others). Understand that this distinction is central to the argument.
3
Understand the implications for rational inquiry
Rational inquiry can only address necessary will. Since the world's existence is a matter of choice, reason cannot prove whether it is eternal or temporal; both are possible.
This is why Aristotle's arguments are called dialectical, not demonstrative.
4
See how the dispute is resolved
The dispute is resolved because neither side can provide a decisive proof. The only way to know is through divine revelation, which states the world is temporal.
The editor notes that modern science may also support temporality.
5
Reflect on the editor's comment
Yusuf Karam's comment adds that modern scientific evidence (e.g., entropy) may incline us toward temporality, but this is not a rational proof either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سبق القول بأن الله لا يريد بالضرورة إلا ذاته ، وأنه يريد غيره بالاختيار ، فهو ليس يريد بالضرورة ، أن يكون العالم ، لا أن يكون قديماً ، ولا أن يكون حادثاً . وهكذا يحسم الخلاف الطويل العنيف بين أنصار القدم وأنصار الحدوث ، ذلك بأن البحث العقلى فى الإرادة الإلهية ، لا يمكن أن يتناول سوى الإرادة الضرورية ؛ أما الاختيار ، فليس يكشف عنه سوى الله ، وقد فعل ؛ إذا أوحى أن العالم حادث . ولكن من جهة العقل البحت ، القدم والحدوث ممكنان على السواء، ولا سبيل إلى إقامة البرهان على ضرورة أحد الحدين وإسقاط الآخر ؛ فلئن كان الله – منذ الأزل علة كافية للعالم ، وكان فاعلا بذاته ، على ما يقول أنصار القدم ؛ إلا أنه ليس يلزم من ذلك جعل العالم صادراً عنه إلا بحسب ما استقر في إرادته . أما أدلة أرسطو فليست برهانية . وقد صرح هو في كتاب الجدل بأن مسألة قدم العالم من المسائل الجدلية . كذلك ليس يمكن إثبات الحدوث بالبرهان ، لا من جهة الله كما أسلفنا ، ولا من جهة العالم ؛ فإن الماهيات مجردة عن خصوص المكان والزمان ، فليس يمكن أن نثبت حدوث الإنسان أو السماء أو الحجر ) ويعلق الأستاذ يوسف كرم على هذه الحجة بقوله : ( وهذه الحجة فيها نظر ، والعلم الراهن يحسب عمر الأرض وعمر الإنسان على سطح الأرض ، وبنوع خاص يقول بتناقص الطاقة ، مما قد يميل بنا إلى نظرية الحدوث .
And it was already stated that God does not will necessarily except His essence, and that He wills others by choice, so He does not necessarily will the world to exist, neither that it be eternal, nor that it be temporal. Thus the long violent dispute between the supporters of eternity and the supporters of temporality is resolved, because rational inquiry into the divine will can only address the necessary will; as for choice, only God reveals it, and He has done so, since He revealed that the world is temporal. But from the perspective of pure reason, eternity and temporality are both possible, and there is no way to establish a proof for the necessity of one of the two and the refutation of the other. For even if God—from eternity—was a sufficient cause for the world and an agent by essence, as the supporters of eternity say, it does not necessarily follow from that that the world emanates from Him except according to what is established in His will. As for Aristotle's arguments, they are not demonstrative. He himself stated in the book of Topics that the question of the eternity of the world is among dialectical questions. Likewise, temporality cannot be proven by demonstration, neither from God's side as we have said, nor from the world's side, because essences are abstracted from particularity of place and time, so we cannot prove the temporality of man, or the heaven, or the stone. And Professor Yusuf Karam comments on this argument saying: 'This argument is questionable, and modern science calculates the age of the earth and the age of man on the earth's surface, and in particular speaks of the decrease of energy, which may incline us toward the theory of temporality.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents Aquinas's resolution of the debate on the world's eternity. It states that God necessarily wills only His own essence; He wills other things by choice, so He does not necessarily will the world to exist, nor that it be eternal or temporal. This resolves the dispute because rational inquiry can only address necessary will, while choice is revealed only by God, and He has revealed that the world is temporal. From pure reason, both eternity and temporality are possible; no proof can establish one over the other. Even if God is an eternal sufficient cause, it does not necessitate emanation except as per His will. Aristotle's proofs are not demonstrative; he admitted the question is dialectical. Temporality cannot be proven by demonstration from God's side or from the world's side, because essences are abstracted from space and time. The editor then includes Yusuf Karam's comment suggesting that modern science may support temporality.

الاختيار
القدم
الحدوث
البرهان
المسائل الجدلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rational arguments can decisively prove whether the world is eternal or temporal.
Correction: The passage argues that both sides are rationally possible; only revelation can settle the matter.

Checkpoint

B) Because choice is not open to rational demonstration and is only revealed by God.
C) Because both sides have equally strong proofs.
D) Because the world is necessarily eternal.

Key Takeaway
The debate on the world's eternity is resolved by recognizing that divine choice, not rational proof, determines the matter, and that only revelation can inform us of God's choice.

Divine Will and the Limits of Reason in Proving Eternity or Temporality
Aquinas argues God's choice makes both eternity and temporality possible, not provable by reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to this passage, human reason cannot definitively prove whether the world is eternal or temporal, and why the matter is settled only by divine revelation.

Core Idea
Reason leaves both eternity and temporality possible; only revelation decides.

This passage explores a central debate in Islamic theology: can reason alone determine whether the world is eternal or created in time? Drawing on the distinction between God's necessary will for Himself and His free choice for creation, the author argues that both positions are rationally possible and that no demonstrative proof exists for either. Only divine revelation resolves the question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of divine will
Find where the author distinguishes between necessary will (الإرادة الضرورية) and free choice (الاختيار). Notice that God necessarily wills only Himself, and wills creation by choice.
This distinction is key to the whole argument.
3
Understand why reason cannot prove either eternity or temporality
The author states that rational investigation only deals with necessary will; free choice is not accessible to reason. Therefore, from pure reason, both eternity and temporality are possible, and no demonstration can force one.
Also note the reference to Aristotle's admission that the question is dialectical.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
The author concludes that only God can disclose His choice, which He did by revealing that the world is temporal. This shows the limits of reason in theological matters.
Consider how this argument relates to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سبق القول بأن الله لا يريد بالضرورة إلا ذاته، وأنه يريد غيره بالاختيار، فهو ليس يريد بالضرورة أن يكون العالم، لا أن يكون قديماً، ولا أن يكون حادثاً. وهكذا يحسم الخلاف الطويل العنيف بين أنصار القدم وأنصار الحدوث، ذلك بأن البحث العقلي في الإرادة الإلهية، لا يمكن أن يتناول سوى الإرادة الضرورية؛ أما الاختيار، فليس يكشف عنه سوى الله، وقد فعل؛ إذا أوحى أن العالم حادث. ولكن من جهة العقل البحت، القدم والحدوث ممكنان على السواء، ولا سبيل إلى إقامة البرهان على ضرورة أحد الحدين وإسقاط الآخر؛ فلئن كان الله – منذ الأزل علة كافية للعالم، وكان فاعلاً بذاته، على ما يقول أنصار القدم؛ إلا أنه ليس يلزم من ذلك جعل العالم صادراً عنه إلا بحسب ما استقر في إرادته. أما أدلة أرسطو فليست برهانية. وقد صرح هو في كتاب الجدل بأن مسألة قدم العالم من المسائل الجدلية. كذلك ليس يمكن إثبات الحدوث بالبرهان، لا من جهة الله كما أسلفنا، ولا من جهة العالم؛ فإن الماهيات مجردة عن خصوص المكان والزمان، فليس يمكن أن نثبت حدوث الإنسان أو السماء أو الحجر. ويعلق الأستاذ يوسف كرم على هذه الحجة بقوله: (وهذه الحجة فيها نظر، والعلم الراهن يحسب عمر الأرض وعمر الإنسان على سطح الأرض، وبنوع خاص يقول بتناقص الطاقة، مما قد يميل بنا إلى نظرية الحدوث).
It has been previously stated that God does not will necessarily except His own essence, and that He wills other than Himself by choice; so He does not necessarily will that the world should be, neither that it should be eternal, nor that it should be temporal. And thus the long fierce dispute between the proponents of eternity and the proponents of temporality is resolved, because rational investigation of the divine will can only deal with necessary will; as for choice, it is only disclosed by God, and He has done so when He revealed that the world is temporal. But from the perspective of pure reason, eternity and temporality are both possible, and there is no way to establish a demonstration of the necessity of one alternative and to reject the other; for even if God – from eternity was a sufficient cause for the world, and was an agent by His essence, as the proponents of eternity say; yet it does not follow from that that the world proceeds from Him except in accordance with what has settled in His will. As for Aristotle's proofs, they are not demonstrative. He himself stated in his book on dialectics that the question of the eternity of the world is a dialectical question. Likewise, temporality cannot be demonstrated by proof, neither from the side of God as we have said, nor from the side of the world; because essences are abstracted from particularity of place and time, so we cannot prove the temporality of man or the heaven or the stone. And Professor Yusuf Karam comments on this argument by saying: (This argument is questionable, and modern science calculates the age of the earth and the age of man on the surface of the earth, and especially speaks of the diminution of energy, which may incline us toward the theory of temporality.)
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage explains Aquinas's view on divine will and reason's limits. It begins by reiterating that God necessarily wills only Himself, and He wills creation by free choice, so the world's existence is not necessary. Thus, the dispute between those who argue for eternity (قدم) and those for temporality (حدوث) is resolved: reason can only discuss necessary will, not free choice. Pure reason finds both possibilities equally viable; no proof can force either. Aquinas notes even Aristotle admitted the question is dialectical. Furthermore, the temporality of specific things cannot be proven because essences are abstract from time and place. The passage ends with a modern scholar's comment that contemporary science might support temporality.

Correction: Reason shows both are possible; only revelation provides the answer.

Third Argument: Equality with God in Duration
Dialectical argument on eternity and duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the eternity of the world and its refutation based on the distinction between God's timeless existence and the world's temporal succession.

Core Idea
God's eternity is not a duration; He exists outside time entirely.

The third argument from the supporters of temporal creation claims that if the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in duration, implying a problematic partnership. In response, the philosophers who affirm the world's eternity make a crucial distinction: God's existence is a single, timeless act, while the world's existence unfolds in successive moments. This refutation does not deny the world's eternity but redefines what eternity means for God versus creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two opposing sides: the supporters of ḥudūth (temporal creation) and the supporters of qidam (pre-eternity). Understand their claims.
The passage is a compact dialectical exchange.
2
Analyze the core distinction
The refutation hinges on the difference between simultaneous existence (all at once) and sequential existence (by succession). God's existence is timeless and simultaneous; the world's existence is temporal and successive.
This distinction removes any true equality in duration.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: Does God experience time? Why is it important that God's eternity be understood as qualitatively different from a long-lasting world? This protects divine transcendence and uniqueness.
This argument shows that even if the world is eternal, it does not rival God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أنصار الحدوث : لو كان العالم قديماً ، لكان مساوياً لله في المدة و يرد أنصار القدم : إن الوجود الإلهى حاصل كله دفعة واحدة ، ووجود العالم حاصل بالتعاقب ، فليست هناك مساواة .
The supporters of ḥudūth say: If the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in duration. And the supporters of qidam respond: The divine existence occurs all at once, while the world's existence occurs successively, so there is no equality.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for temporal creation and its refutation. The supporters of temporal creation argue that if the world is eternal, it would be co-eternal with God, implying a partnership in duration. The supporters of pre-eternity reply that God's existence is a single timeless reality, whereas the world's existence is a temporal succession. God does not exist 'in time' in the same way, so there is no equality. Duration is not applicable to God in the same sense as to the world.

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
مساو في المدة (musāwin fī al-mudda)
وجود بالتعاقب (wujūd bi-l-ta‘āqub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that eternity simply means 'existing for a very long time' and that two eternal beings would automatically share the same temporal duration.
Correction: God's eternity is not temporal; it is timeless. The world's eternity, if it exists, is temporal succession without beginning. These are qualitatively different, so no equality in duration arises.

Checkpoint
How do the supporters of pre-eternity refute the claim that an eternal world would equal God in duration?
A) They deny that the world is eternal.
B) They say that God's existence is not in time, while the world's existence is successive.
C) They argue that equality does not matter.
D) They claim that the world is not independent of God.

Key Takeaway
Eternity does not mean 'existing for a very long time'; God's eternity is without succession, making it incomparable to the world's temporal extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of God as existing for an infinite amount of time. But the lesson says His existence is not a duration at all. How does that change the way you relate to a God who is not bound by the time you experience every day? What comfort or challenge does this bring you?
?
If God's existence is 'all at once' without past or future, then your entire life is present to Him. How does that awareness affect the sin you committed yesterday or the worry you have about tomorrow? What step will you take today to live as though you are truly seen in this timeless present?
?
You may have thought of eternity as just a very long time. But now you see that God's eternity is qualitatively different — not stretched out like yours. Does this make God feel more distant or more intimate? Why? And what does that reveal about your assumptions of who He is?

The Philosophers' Objection: Eternal Will and Temporal Events
Ghazali restates the philosophers' objection and the dilemma of an eternal will causing temporal events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that an eternal will cannot produce temporal events without undergoing change, and the dilemma it leads to.

Core Idea
An unchanging eternal will cannot cause change in time without contradiction.

Ghazali begins by presenting the philosophers' objection against the idea that an eternal will could create the world in time. They argue that if the will is eternal and unchanging, any temporal effect would require a renewed intention, implying change in the eternal — which they deem impossible. This sets up the central problem that Ghazali will address.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Read the passage carefully to grasp the philosophers' claim: an eternal will like human determination is insufficient; a renewed intentional impulse is needed, which would change the eternal.
Notice that they equate the eternal will with human determination.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the renewed impulse is an uncaused event, or it leads to infinite regress. Understand why both are considered problematic.
3
Recognize the summary of their position
Ghazali summarizes the philosophers' view: a complete cause that exists for eternity without effect would suddenly produce an effect without any new condition — which they hold as impossible.
This summary is crucial for understanding what Ghazali will challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى قيام في الغد إلا بطريق العزم ، وإن كانت الإرادة القديمة في حكم عزمنا ، فليس ذلك كافياً في وقوع المعزوم عليه ، بل لابد من تجدد انبعاث قصدى عند الإيجاد وفيه قول بتغير القديم، ثم يبقى عين الإشكال في أن ذلك الانبعاث أو القصد أو الإرادة أو ما شئت فسمه ، لم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل ذلك فإما أن يبقى حادث بلا سبب أو يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية . • ورجع حاصل الكلام إلى أن وجد الموجب بتمام شروطه ولم يبق أمر منتظر ، ذلك تأخر الموجب ولم يوجد في مدة لا يرتقى الوهم إلى أولها ، بل آلاف سنين ومع لا تنقص منها شيئاً ، ثم انقلب الموجب بغتة من غير أمر تجدد ، وشرط تحقق ، محال في نفسه وهو والجواب : أن يقال : استحالة إرادة قديمة متعلقة بأحداث شيء ، أي شيء كان ، تعرفونه بضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟ ! وعلى لغتكم في المنطق ، تعرفون الالتقاء بين هذين الحدين بحد أوسط أو من غير حد أوسط ؟ فإن ادعيتم حداً أوسط وهو الطريق النظرى ، فلابد من إظهاره ، وإن ادعيتم معرفة ذلك ضرورة، فكيف لم يشارككم في معرفته مخالفوكم ، والفرقة المعتقدة لحدوث العالم بإرادة قديمة ، لا يحصرها بلد ، ولا يحصيها عدد ، ولاشك في أنهم لا يكابرون العقول عناداً مع المعرفة ؛ فلابد من إقامة برهان على شرط المنطق يدل على استحالة ذلك ؛ إذ ليس فى جميع ما ذكرتموه إلا الاستعباد والتمثيل بعزمنا وإرادتنا ، وهو فاسد ، فلا تضاهى الإرادة القديمة القصود الحادثة ، وأما الاستعباد المجرد فلا يكفي من غير برهان
... except by way of determination. And if the eternal will is like our determination, that is not sufficient for the occurrence of the determined thing; rather there must be a renewal of intentional impulse at the time of bringing into existence, and in that there is a saying of change in the eternal. Then the very problem remains: that impulse, or intention, or will, or whatever you call it, why did it occur now and not before? So either it is an event without a cause, or it leads to infinite regress. • The summary of the discourse is that the sufficient cause existed with all its conditions and nothing remained pending, that the cause was delayed and did not exist for a period that the imagination cannot reach its beginning, even thousands of years, and nothing was missing from it, then the cause suddenly turned without any new thing, a condition realized, impossible in itself. And the answer is to say: The impossibility of an eternal will being related to temporal events, any events whatsoever, do you know it by necessity of reason or by theoretical reasoning? And in your logic, do you know the connection between these two terms by a middle term or without a middle term? If you claim a middle term, which is the theoretical path, then you must produce it. And if you claim knowledge of that by necessity, then why do your opponents not share with you in that knowledge? The group that believes in the temporal creation of the world by an eternal will is not confined to one country nor can it be counted; and there is no doubt that they are not obstinately disputing the intellects with knowledge; so you must produce a demonstration on the conditions of logic indicating the impossibility of that, for all that you have mentioned is only an expression of impossibility and analogy with our determination and will, and that is invalid. For the eternal will is not comparable to temporal intentions. And mere expression of impossibility is not sufficient without demonstration.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' objection that an eternal will cannot cause temporal events without undergoing change. Ghazali first restates the problem: if the eternal will is like human determination, then a renewed impulse is needed for creation, which implies change in the eternal, or else the event would be uncaused or lead to an infinite regress. He then summarizes the philosophers' claim: a complete cause existing without effect is impossible. Ghazali responds by challenging them to prove that impossibility—do they know it by necessity or by demonstration? If by demonstration, they must produce a logical proof; if by necessity, then why do many rational people not share that intuition? He notes that many Muslims believe in creation by an eternal will without seeing impossibility. Hence, mere analogy with human will is insufficient; a rigorous demonstration is required.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ghazali accepts the philosophers' premise that a complete cause must produce its effect immediately.
Correction: Ghazali is merely reporting their position; he will later challenge its validity and demand a logical proof.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali report as the philosophers' view about a complete cause with all its conditions?
A) It must produce its effect immediately.
B) It can delay its effect without any new condition.
C) It requires a renewed intention.
D) It leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that an eternal will cannot cause temporal events without change, leading to either an uncaused event or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to think that God's will must operate exactly like human will? In what area of your life does this assumption affect how you trust His decree? How can you remind yourself that His ways are higher than yours, starting today?
?
When you encounter a logical difficulty in understanding God's attributes, do you quickly reject it as impossible, or do you humbly acknowledge the limit of human reason? Which response does this dilemma in the philosophers' argument expose in your own heart? Commit to one concrete action this week that reflects trust in God's wisdom over your understanding.
?
If an eternal, unchanging will cannot cause temporal events according to the philosophers, then how do you explain your own experience of answered prayers or divine intervention? Does this dilemma challenge you to seek God's guidance more earnestly, or does it shake your certainty? What step can you take today to strengthen your reliance on God's power despite intellectual puzzles?

Ghazali's Response: Demand for a Logical Proof
Ghazali challenges the philosophers to prove their claim by necessity or demonstration, and dismisses mere analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method of refutation: challenging the philosophers to prove the impossibility of an eternal will causing temporal events through a logical demonstration, not mere analogy.

Core Idea
The philosophers must produce a proof, not just an analogy with human will.

Having presented the philosophers' objection, Ghazali now responds by questioning the basis of their claim. He asks whether they know the impossibility by necessity or by demonstration, and then points out that many rational people disagree with them. This forces the philosophers to either produce a rigorous logical proof or admit their argument is merely analogical and thus insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's initial challenge
Start by reading the passage where Ghazali asks: do you know the impossibility by necessity of reason or by theoretical reasoning?
This sets up a dichotomy that structures his response.
2
Consider the dilemma
If by demonstration, they must produce a middle term. If by necessity, why don't others share that intuition? Understand the force of this argument.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that mere analogy with human will and expressions of impossibility are not sufficient; a logical demonstration on the conditions of logic is required.
This is the core of his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى قيام في الغد إلا بطريق العزم ، وإن كانت الإرادة القديمة في حكم عزمنا ، فليس ذلك كافياً في وقوع المعزوم عليه ، بل لابد من تجدد انبعاث قصدى عند الإيجاد وفيه قول بتغير القديم، ثم يبقى عين الإشكال في أن ذلك الانبعاث أو القصد أو الإرادة أو ما شئت فسمه ، لم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل ذلك فإما أن يبقى حادث بلا سبب أو يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية . • ورجع حاصل الكلام إلى أن وجد الموجب بتمام شروطه ولم يبق أمر منتظر ، ذلك تأخر الموجب ولم يوجد في مدة لا يرتقى الوهم إلى أولها ، بل آلاف سنين ومع لا تنقص منها شيئاً ، ثم انقلب الموجب بغتة من غير أمر تجدد ، وشرط تحقق ، محال في نفسه وهو والجواب : أن يقال : استحالة إرادة قديمة متعلقة بأحداث شيء ، أي شيء كان ، تعرفونه بضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟ ! وعلى لغتكم في المنطق ، تعرفون الالتقاء بين هذين الحدين بحد أوسط أو من غير حد أوسط ؟ فإن ادعيتم حداً أوسط وهو الطريق النظرى ، فلابد من إظهاره ، وإن ادعيتم معرفة ذلك ضرورة، فكيف لم يشارككم في معرفته مخالفوكم ، والفرقة المعتقدة لحدوث العالم بإرادة قديمة ، لا يحصرها بلد ، ولا يحصيها عدد ، ولاشك في أنهم لا يكابرون العقول عناداً مع المعرفة ؛ فلابد من إقامة برهان على شرط المنطق يدل على استحالة ذلك ؛ إذ ليس فى جميع ما ذكرتموه إلا الاستعباد والتمثيل بعزمنا وإرادتنا ، وهو فاسد ، فلا تضاهى الإرادة القديمة القصود الحادثة ، وأما الاستعباد المجرد فلا يكفي من غير برهان
... except by way of determination. And if the eternal will is like our determination, that is not sufficient for the occurrence of the determined thing; rather there must be a renewal of intentional impulse at the time of bringing into existence, and in that there is a saying of change in the eternal. Then the very problem remains: that impulse, or intention, or will, or whatever you call it, why did it occur now and not before? So either it is an event without a cause, or it leads to infinite regress. • The summary of the discourse is that the sufficient cause existed with all its conditions and nothing remained pending, that the cause was delayed and did not exist for a period that the imagination cannot reach its beginning, even thousands of years, and nothing was missing from it, then the cause suddenly turned without any new thing, a condition realized, impossible in itself. And the answer is to say: The impossibility of an eternal will being related to temporal events, any events whatsoever, do you know it by necessity of reason or by theoretical reasoning? And in your logic, do you know the connection between these two terms by a middle term or without a middle term? If you claim a middle term, which is the theoretical path, then you must produce it. And if you claim knowledge of that by necessity, then why do your opponents not share with you in that knowledge? The group that believes in the temporal creation of the world by an eternal will is not confined to one country nor can it be counted; and there is no doubt that they are not obstinately disputing the intellects with knowledge; so you must produce a demonstration on the conditions of logic indicating the impossibility of that, for all that you have mentioned is only an expression of impossibility and analogy with our determination and will, and that is invalid. For the eternal will is not comparable to temporal intentions. And mere expression of impossibility is not sufficient without demonstration.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' objection that an eternal will cannot cause temporal events without undergoing change. Ghazali first restates the problem: if the eternal will is like human determination, then a renewed impulse is needed for creation, which implies change in the eternal, or else the event would be uncaused or lead to an infinite regress. He then summarizes the philosophers' claim: a complete cause existing without effect is impossible. Ghazali responds by challenging them to prove that impossibility—do they know it by necessity or by demonstration? If by demonstration, they must produce a logical proof; if by necessity, then why do many rational people not share that intuition? He notes that many Muslims believe in creation by an eternal will without seeing impossibility. Hence, mere analogy with human will is insufficient; a rigorous demonstration is required.

الاستعباد
البرهان
الحد الأوسط

Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is simply asserting that an eternal will can cause temporal events without proof.
Correction: Ghazali does not assert that; he instead demands that the philosophers prove their impossibility claim, shifting the burden of proof.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say the philosophers must produce to prove the impossibility of an eternal will causing temporal events?
A) An analogy with human will
B) A demonstration on the conditions of logic
C) A consensus of all philosophers
D) A scriptural proof

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers by challenging them to prove their claim by logical demonstration, dismissing mere analogy as insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you ever unconsciously compare it to your own will—like assuming God must act within the same limitations you experience? This passage declares such analogy invalid. Where in your life have you made this error, and how can you actively train yourself to remember God's incomparability?
?
Ghazali places the burden of proof on those who claim something is impossible for God. Have you ever dismissed a theological possibility (like God creating the world in a certain way) simply because it seemed unlikely to your finite mind? What is one belief about God that you hold without evidence, and how would Ghazali's challenge push you to seek proper demonstration?
?
The philosophers used an analogy to argue against the eternal will. Have you ever used a human analogy to explain or defend a point about God—perhaps in a discussion with a friend? After reading this passage, are you willing to examine that argument critically? Commit to finding one weakness in your analogy and seeking a better, more direct proof.

Objection and Counter-Objection on Necessary Knowledge
Ghazali presents and counters the philosophers' objection on necessary causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own doctrine of God's knowledge to counter their claim that causality is known by necessity of reason.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the philosophers' argument against themselves.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a philosophical objection and responds with a powerful rebuttal. The philosophers argue that a complete cause without its effect is rationally impossible. Ghazali counters by pointing out an inconsistency: their own view of God's knowledge violates the same rational standard, yet they accept it. This reveals the arbitrariness of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the philosophers' claim that a sufficient cause without its effect is inconceivable by necessity of reason.
Notice how they frame this as a self-evident rational truth.
2
Read Ghazali's counter
Read Ghazali's response where he refers to the philosophers' own doctrine of God's knowledge of all universals without multiplicity.
He is drawing an analogy to show inconsistency.
3
Identify the inconsistency
Understand why the philosophers cannot consistently hold both positions: one applying necessary reason to causality but exempting divine knowledge.
This is the core of Ghazali's argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this dialectical move is important in the broader debate about causality and divine attributes.
It undermines the philosophers' appeal to self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن بضرورة العقل نعلم أنه لا يتصور موجب بتمام شروطه من غير موجب ، ومجوز ذلك مكابر لضرورة العقل قلنا : وما الفصل بينكم وبين خصومكم ، إذا قالوا لكم : إنا بالضرورة نعلم إحالة قول من يقول : إن ذاتاً واحدة عالمة بجميع الكليات من غير أن يوجب ذلك كثرة ، ومن غير أن يكون العلم زيادة على الذات ، ومن غير أن يتعدد العلم مع تعدد المعلوم ، وهذا مذهبكم في حق الله ، وهو بالنسبة إلينا وإلى علومنا في غاية الإحالة ، ولكن تقولون : لا يقاس العلم القديم بالحادث
If it is said: 'We know by necessity of reason that a sufficient cause with all its conditions without an effect is inconceivable, and one who permits that is obstinate against necessary reason.' We say: 'What is the difference between you and your opponents when they say to you: We know by necessity the impossibility of the statement that a single essence knows all universals without that entailing multiplicity, and without knowledge being an addition to the essence, and without knowledge being multiple with the multiplicity of the known? This is your doctrine regarding God, and it is in the extreme of impossibility relative to us and to our sciences. But you say: The eternal knowledge is not to be measured by the temporal.'
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The philosophers object that it is known by necessity of reason that a complete cause cannot exist without its effect; to believe otherwise is to contradict necessary reason. Ghazali responds by turning the argument back on them: their own doctrine of God's knowledge is equally impossible by human standards—God knows all universals without multiplicity, without knowledge being an attribute distinct from His essence, without multiple knowledges for multiple objects. By their own logic, this should be rejected as impossible. But they exempt God's knowledge from such analogies. Ghazali thus exposes inconsistency: they insist on necessary ruling for causality but abandon it for divine knowledge.

العلم القديم

Correction: Ghazali is not accepting it; he is showing that the same standard would invalidate their own doctrine, revealing inconsistency.

?
Ghazali forces his opponents to see the inconsistency in their own reasoning. Is there a cherished belief or habit in your life that, when examined closely, violates a principle you insist others follow? Will you honestly confront that inconsistency?
?
The philosophers exempted divine knowledge from the rational necessity they applied to causality. Do you ever make exceptions for yourself in matters of faith or morality, claiming a special status that you would not grant to others? What would change if you removed that exception starting now?

Refutation of the Philosophers' Claim on Divine Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that God knows all universals without multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that God knows all universals without multiplicity, and his argument against the identification of God with intellect/intelligent/intelligible.

Core Idea
God must know His creation; denying that leads to absurdity.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa challenges the philosophers' view of divine knowledge. They claimed God knows all universals without multiplicity in His essence. Ghazali exposes the inherent contradictions and shows that such a view ultimately denies God's knowledge of His own creation, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers assert that God as a single essence knows all universals without multiplicity of knowledge, and that knowledge is not an addition to the essence.
This is their doctrine regarding God.
2
Notice Ghazali's initial objection
Ghazali states that this claim is impossible when compared to human knowledge, but the philosophers respond that eternal knowledge is not measured by temporal.
This is a common defense.
3
Examine the faction's alternative
Some philosophers, sensing the impossibility, claimed God knows only Himself, making intellect/intelligent/intelligible one.
This is an attempt to avoid multiplicity.
4
Understand Ghazali's refutation of this alternative
Ghazali argues that the union of these three is known by necessity to be impossible, and that a Maker who does not know His craft is also impossible.
This leads to the conclusion that God would not know His creation at all.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali exalts God above these errors, emphasizing that on their view God would not know His craft nor any limitation.
The refutation is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إحالة قول من يقول : إن ذاتاً واحدة عالمة بجميع الكليات من غير أن يوجب ذلك كثرة ، ومن غير أن يكون العلم زيادة على الذات ، ومن غير أن يتعدد العلم مع تعدد المعلوم ، وهذا مذهبكم في حق الله ، وهو بالنسبة إلينا وإلى علومنا في غاية الإحالة ، ولكن تقولون : لا يقاس العلم القديم بالحادث ؛ وطائفة منكم استشعروا إحالة هذا فقالوا : إن الله لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فهو العاقل ، وهو العقل وهو المعقول ، والكل واحد ، فلو قال قائل : اتحاد العقل والعاقل والمعقول معلوم الاستحالة بالضرورة ، إذ تقدير صانع للعالم لا يعلم صنعته محال بالضرورة والقديم إذا لم يعلم إلا نفسه – تعالى عن قولكم وعن قول قولكم وعن قول جميع الزائغين علواً كبيراً – لم يكن يعلم صنعته ألبتة ولا حصر
The impossibility of the statement of one who says: A single essence knowing all universals without that necessitating multiplicity, and without knowledge being an addition to the essence, and without knowledge being multiplied with the multiplication of the known — and this is your doctrine concerning God, and it is with respect to us and our sciences in the utmost impossibility. But you say: The eternal knowledge is not measured by the temporal. And a faction among you sensing the impossibility of this said: God knows only Himself, so He is the intellect, and He is the intelligent, and He is the intelligible, and all is one. For if someone said: The union of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible is known by necessity to be impossible; for the supposition of a Maker of the world who does not know His craft is impossible by necessity; and the Eternal, if He knows only Himself — exalted is He above your statement and above the statement of your statement and above the statement of all the deviators with a great exaltation — He would not know His craft at all, nor any limitation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (إحالة) of the philosophers' specific claim about divine knowledge. Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' position: that God as a single essence knows all universals without this causing multiplicity in His essence, without knowledge being an additional attribute, and without knowledge multiplying with the multiplicity of known things. He notes that this is their doctrine regarding God, and while it seems impossible relative to human knowledge, they argue that eternal knowledge is not analogous to temporal knowledge. Then he points out that a faction among them, sensing the impossibility of this, retreated to an even more extreme position: that God knows only Himself, identifying intellect (عقل), intelligent (عاقل), and intelligible (معقول) as one. Ghazali then refutes this by invoking necessary knowledge: the unification of these three is known to be impossible by necessity. Moreover, if God does not know His creation, then the supposition of a Maker who does not know His own work is also impossible by necessity. He concludes by exalting God above their erroneous statements, declaring that on their view God would not know His craft at all, nor any limitation (لا حصر).

Key Terms
إحالة
ذات
كليات
علم قديم
عقل
عاقل
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can know all universals without multiplicity in His essence, and this does not lead to any problem.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this claim leads to the unacceptable conclusion that God does not know His creation, which is impossible for a Maker.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the consequence of the philosophers' claim that God knows only Himself?
A) God's knowledge becomes perfect and unlimited.
B) God would not know His own creation at all.
C) God's knowledge becomes identical with human knowledge.
D) The union of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim about divine knowledge by showing it leads to the impossible conclusion that God does not know His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every single detail of your life—your thoughts, intentions, and hidden actions? If so, how would your life change if you lived with this awareness every moment?
?
When you sin, do you act as if God does not know, or do you remember that He is the Maker who knows His creation perfectly? How can you cultivate this remembrance in your daily choices?
?
The philosophers' claim reduced God's knowledge to something abstract and distant. Have you ever reduced God in your mind—making Him less involved, less knowing, or less personal than He truly is? Commit to correcting one such misconception today.

Objection on the Duration Before Creation and Ghazali's Answer
Philosophers object about the infinite duration before creation and Ghazali answers briefly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection about the duration before creation and Ghazali's initial response that time is created.

Core Idea
Time itself is created, so the problem of a waiting period before creation dissolves.

This passage presents a key objection from the philosophers against the Islamic belief that God created the world at a specific moment in time. They argue that if God could create at any time, His delay implies an absurdity. Ghazali's short answer points to a fundamental insight: time is not absolute but created, thus reframing the entire debate. We will examine the objection and Ghazali's concise reply.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The philosophers argue that if God could create at any time, the delay between His ability and actual creation leads to a dilemma about the duration of waiting.
Notice the phrase 'هذا ينقلب عليكم' — the objection turns the tables on the Islamic position.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Either the duration of waiting is finite (making God's existence finite in the past) or infinite (meaning infinite possibilities elapsed, which is absurd).
This is a classic 'finite or infinite' dilemma.
3
Examine Ghazali's answer
Ghazali states that time and duration are themselves created. Therefore, the dilemma does not apply because there is no pre-existing time to wait in. He promises a fuller refutation later.
This answer is preliminary; the full argument comes in response to the philosophers' second proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا ينقلب عليكم فى أن الله قبل خلق العالم كان قادراً على الخلق بقدر سنة أو سنين ، ولا نهاية لقدرته ، فكأنه صبر ولم يخلق ، ثم خلق ، ومدة الترك متناه أو غير متناه ؟ فإن قلتم : متناه ، صار وجود البارى متناهى الأول ، وإن قلتم : غير متناه ، فقد انقضى مدة فيها إمكانيات لا نهاية لإعدادها . قلنا : المدة والزمان مخلوقان عندنا ، وسنبين حقيقة الجواب عن هذا في الانفصال عن دليلهم الثانى .
If it is said: This turns against you regarding the fact that God before the creation of the world was able to create by a year or years, and there is no end to His power. So it is as if He waited and did not create, then created. Is the duration of abstention finite or infinite? If you say finite, then the existence of the Creator becomes finite in its beginning. If you say infinite, then a duration has elapsed containing possibilities with no end to their number. We say: Duration and time are created according to us, and we will explain the true answer to this in response to their second proof.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an objection presented by the philosophers and Ghazali's preliminary answer. The objection argues that if God could create at any time, the fact that He created at a specific time implies either that He waited a finite time (making His existence finite in the past) or an infinite time (meaning infinite possibilities elapsed, which is absurd). Ghazali's answer is concise: time and duration themselves are created, so the problem does not arise. He defers the full explanation to his refutation of the second proof (from the philosophers). Thus this lesson presents the objection and Ghazali's immediate response, without the full argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The duration before creation was real, flowing time.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that time and duration are themselves created; there is no pre-existing time. Thus, there is no real 'waiting period' before creation.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali initially respond to the objection about the duration before creation?
A) He argues that God's power is limited.
B) He says that duration and time are created entities.
C) He admits that the objection is valid and changes his position.
D) He claims that creation happened in a finite past.

Key Takeaway
Time is a created entity, so there is no real duration before creation; the philosophers' dilemma is based on a false assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if time is an independent container that limits God's action, or do you truly believe that every moment is directly created by Him? When you feel impatient, are you aware that your very sense of time is a creation of God?
?
If time itself is created, then your past sins are not trapped in an unchangeable timeline but are erased by repentance. How does this reality affect your urgency to repent today, knowing that there is no 'past' separate from God's creation?
?
When you worry about the future, are you assuming that future time already exists and God is 'waiting' to act? How can you train your heart to see that the future is as non-existent as the time before creation, and that God's power is immediate?
