The Philosophers' Method of Reducing Divine Names
Ghazali explains the reduction of divine names to negation or relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' method of reducing divine names to either negation (salb) or relation (idafa), and how this preserves divine unity.

Core Idea
Every divine name is either a negation, a relation, or a combination.

The philosophers were accused of denying all positive divine attributes. Ghazali here explains their actual position: they accept many names but interpret them as logical relations or negations that do not compromise the oneness of God's essence. This passage lays out the method with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core principle
Read the opening where Ghazali insists on clarifying the philosophers' doctrine before objecting. The key is that the essence is one; names multiply only via negation or relation.
Negation and relation do not add real multiplicity to the essence.
2
Examine each reduction
Go through the list of names: First, Principle, Existent, Substance, Eternal, Permanent, and Necessary Existent. For each, note whether it is a negation, relation, or combination.
Pay special attention to 'Necessary Existent' which combines both.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this reduction claims to preserve God's absolute oneness while still allowing a rich vocabulary of names.
This is a central point of controversy between philosophers and theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فينبغي أن نحقق مذهبهم للتفهيم أولا ، ثم نشتغل بالاعتراض ، فإن الاعتراض على المذهب ، قبل تمام التفهيم ، رمى في عماية والعمدة في فهم مذهبهم ، أنهم يقولون : ذات المبدأ الأولى واحدة ، وإنما تكثر الأسماء بإضافة شيء إليه ، أو إضافته إلى شيء ، أو سلب شيء عنه ، والسلب لا يوجب كثرة في الذات المسلوب عنه ، ولا الإضافة توجب كثرة

فلا ينكرون إذن كثرة السلوب ، وكثرة الإضافات ، ولكن الشأن في رد هذه الأمور كلها إلى السلب والإضافة . فقالوا : إذا قيل له : ( أول ) ، فهو إضافة إلى الموجودات بعده ، وإذا قيل ( مبدأ ) ، فهو إشارة إلى أن وجود غيره منه ، وهو سبب له ، فهو إضافة له إلى معلولاته ، وإذا قيل : (موجود) ، فمعناه : معلوم ، وإذا قيل : (جوهر ) فمعناه الوجود ، مسلوباً عنه الحلول في موضوع ، وهذا سلب ، وإذا قيل : ( قديم ) ، فمعناه سلب العدم عنه أولاً ، وإذا قيل : ( باق ) ، فمعناه سلب العدم عند آخراً ، فيرجع حاصل القديم والباقى ، إلى أن الوجود ليس مسبوقاً بعدم ، ولا ملحوقاً بعدم وإذا قيل : ( واجب الوجود ) ، فمعناه أنه لا علة له ، وهو علة لغيره ، فيكون جمعاً بين السلب والإضافة ، إذ نفى علة له ، سلب ؛ وجعله علة لغيره ، إضافة ، وإذا قيل ( عقل ) ، فمعناه موجود برىء من المادة ، وكل موجود هذه صفته ، فهو عقل ، أي يعقل ويشعر بها ، ويعقل غيره ، وذات الله تعالى هذه صفتها ، أى هو برىء عن المادة ، فإذن هو عقل ، وهما عبارتان (۱) عن معنى واحد ، وإذا قيل : ( عاقل ) ، فمعناه أن ذاته الذي هو عقل ، له معقول ، هو ذاته ، فإنه يشعر بنفسه ، ويعقل نفسه ، فذاته معقول ، وذاته عاقل ، وذاته عقل ، والكل واحد ، إذ هو ( معقول ) ، من حيث إنه ماهية مجردة عن غير مستورة عن ذاته ، الذي هو عقل ، بمعنى أنه ماهية مجردة عن المادة ، لا يكون شيء مستوراً عنه ، ولما عقل نفسه ، كان عاقلا ، ولما كان نفسه معقولاً لنفسه ، كان معقولاً ، ولما كان عقله بذاته ، لا بزائد على ذاته ، كان عقلاً ، ولا يبعد أن يتحد العاقل (۲) والمعقول ، فإن العاقل إذا عقل كونه عاقلا عقله : « كونه عاقلاً فيكون العاقل والمعقول واحداً بوجه وإن كان عقلنا يفارق عقل الأول ، فإن ما للأول بالفعل أبداً ، وما لنا يكون بالقوة تارة ، وبالفعل تارة أخرى . وإذا قيل : ( خالق ) ، ( وفاعل) ،

(1) لعله يعنى ) يعقل ذاته » و « يعقل غيره » فردها جميعاً إلى أنه «عقل» .
( ۲ ) وفى نسخة « العقل والمعقول » .
We should first verify their doctrine for clarification, then engage in objection. For objecting to a doctrine before complete clarification is shooting in the dark. The key to understanding their doctrine is that they say: the essence of the First Principle is one, and names become multiple only by adding something to it, or adding it to something, or negating something from it. Negation does not cause multiplicity in the essence from which something is negated, nor does addition cause multiplicity. So they do not deny multiplicity of negations and multiplicity of additions. But the task is to reduce all these matters to negation and addition. They said: when 'First' is said of Him, it is an addition to the existents after Him. When 'Principle' is said, it indicates that the existence of other than Him is from Him, and He is a cause for it, so it is an addition of Him to His effects. When 'Existent' is said, its meaning is 'known'. When 'Substance' is said, its meaning is existence with being denied inherence in a subject—this is negation. When 'Eternal' is said, its meaning is negation of non-existence from Him in the beginning. When 'Permanent' is said, its meaning is negation of non-existence at the end. So the outcome of eternal and permanent is that existence is not preceded by non-existence nor followed by non-existence. When 'Necessary Existent' is said, its meaning is that He has no cause and He is a cause for other than Him, so it combines negation and addition: the negation of a cause for Him is negation, and His being a cause for other than Him is addition. When 'Intellect' is said, its meaning is an existence free from matter, and every existent with this description is an intellect, i.e., it intellects and perceives itself and intellects other than itself. The essence of God most high has this description, i.e., He is free from matter, so He is an intellect. And the two expressions (1) are about one meaning. When 'Intelligent' is said, its meaning is that His essence which is Intellect has an intelligible which is His essence. For He perceives Himself and intellects Himself. So His essence is intelligible, His essence is intelligent, and His essence is intellect, and all is one. For He is intelligible insofar as He is a quiddity abstracted from other, not veiled from His essence which is intellect, meaning that as a quiddity abstracted from matter, nothing is veiled from Him. Since He intellects Himself, He is intelligent. Since His self is intelligible to Himself, He is intelligible. Since His intellect is by His essence, not by something additional to His essence, He is intellect. And it is not far that the intelligent and the intelligible become one, for the intelligent when he intellects his being intelligent intellection: 'his being intelligent', so the intelligent and the intelligible become one in one respect, even though our intellect differs from the First's intellect, for what belongs to the First is always actual, and what belongs to us is sometimes potential and sometimes actual. And when 'Creator' and 'Agent' are said...

Footnote (1): Perhaps he means 'intellects His essence' and 'intellects other than Him,' and he refers all of that to 'Intellect.'
Footnote (2): In another copy: 'the Intellect and the Intelligible.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the philosophers' method for reducing all divine names to either negation (salb) or relation (idafa). Ghazali begins by stressing the importance of understanding their position before criticizing it. The core principle: the essence of the First Principle is absolutely one; names only multiply by adding something to Him (i.e., relating Him to something else), adding Him to something (i.e., His relation to others), or negating something from Him. Negation and relation do not introduce multiplicity in the essence itself. Then he lists specific reductions:

- 'First' is a relation to what comes after.
- 'Principle' (Mabda') indicates His being the cause of others, a relation to effects.
- 'Existent' (Mawjud) is interpreted as 'known' (ma'lum), perhaps implying a relational sense.
- 'Substance' (Jawhar) means existence with negation of inherence in a subject—a negation.
- 'Eternal' (Qadim) negates non-existence at the beginning.
- 'Permanent' (Baqi) negates non-existence at the end.
- 'Necessary Existent' (Wajib al-Wujud) combines negation (no cause for Him) and relation (He is cause for others).
- 'Intellect' ('Aql) means existence free from matter; God is such, so He is Intellect.
- 'Intelligent' ('Aqil) means His essence intellects itself.
- 'Intelligible' (Ma'qul) means His essence is intellectible to itself. These three are identical in Him.
- The explanation includes a brief comparison with human intellect, which is potential then actual, while God's intellect is always actual.
- The passage ends with the beginning of the reduction for 'Creator' and 'Agent' (the text is cut off).

إضافة

باق
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers deny all positive divine attributes entirely.
Correction: They reinterpret attributes as negations or relations, not deny them. They accept names like 'First', 'Eternal', etc., but as relationships or negations, not as real attributes added to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, how is the name 'Eternal' (Qadim) interpreted?
A) As a relation to time
B) As negation of non-existence at the beginning
C) As a positive attribute of perpetual duration
D) As both negation and relation

Key Takeaway
The philosophers reinterpret divine names as negations or relations to preserve the absolute oneness of the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call God 'Eternal' (al-Qadim), do you feel the weight of denying any beginning to His existence, or do you treat it as a casual label? How can you let this name transform your sense of time and urgency in seeking Him?
?
The philosophers reduce divine names to avoid multiplicity in God. But in your heart, do you reduce God to mere concepts — 'First' as a priority, 'Existent' as a fact — rather than living in awe of the One who is beyond all categories? Which of these names do you need to experience more intimately today?
?
If 'Existent' meant only 'known' to you, would your worship deepen or shrink? How does the realization that God's reality surpasses human definitions compel you to approach Him with greater humility and love?

The Unity of Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible in God
Ghazali explains the philosophers' identification of God as Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible, and their unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers identify God as Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible, and how these are one in essence.

Core Idea
In God, intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are identical.

The philosophers go further in reducing divine names: they see God as pure intellect because He is free from matter. This leads to the striking claim that God's intellect, that which intellects, and that which is intellectible are one and the same. Ghazali explains this reasoning and contrasts it with human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Define 'Intellect' as free from matter
Read the passage where 'Intellect' ('Aql) is defined as an existence free from matter. This applies to God, so He is Intellect.
Everything free from matter is an intellect that intellects itself.
2
See the triple identity
Notice how the same essence is called Intellect (as a quiddity abstracted), Intelligent (as intellecting itself), and Intelligible (as being intellectible to itself). They are not separate.
The passage emphasizes that these three are one.
3
Compare with human intellect
Observe the contrast: God's intellect is always actual, while human intellect is sometimes potential, sometimes actual. This underscores God's perfection.
The difference is due to matter in humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible are three distinct entities or attributes in God.
Correction: The philosophers hold that they are identical; each term describes a single essence from a different perspective (abstract, agent of intellection, object of intellection).

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is God called 'Intellect'?
A) Because He knows all things
B) Because He is free from matter
C) Because He is the cause of all intellects

Key Takeaway
God is one simple reality described as Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible, all identical, in contrast to the potentiality of human intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in a God who knows you completely, but do you live as if His knowledge is identical with His essence—meaning He never learns about you, but knows you instantly and perfectly? When you sin in secret, do you recognize that there is no gap between God's knowledge and your action? What would change in your life if you truly internalised that God's intellect is always actual, never waiting for you to do something?
?
If God's intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one, then you cannot hide any part of yourself from Him. Is there something you are trying to keep hidden from God right now? Will you expose it to Him in prayer today, trusting that His knowledge of you is a complete and merciful knowledge?
?
You may think of God as having separate 'parts'—His anger, His mercy, His knowledge—but this lesson teaches that in God, even intellect and its object are identical. Are you still thinking of God as a composite being? Examine your heart: do you really believe that God is one simple reality, or do your prayers and thoughts imply division in His essence? Repent of any subtle shirk in your conception of God.

Reduction of Divine Attributes to Negations and Relations
Ghazali reports the philosophers' reduction of essential attributes to negations and relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers reduce each divine attribute to either a negation or a relation, and why this reduction is presented as a way to preserve God's simplicity.

Core Idea
Every divine attribute is either a negation of imperfection or a relation to creation.

Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument that all positive descriptions of God can be reduced to either negations (like 'Eternal' meaning 'not preceded by non-existence') or relations (like 'First' meaning 'related to what comes after'). This reduction aims to preserve God's simplicity while still allowing meaningful discourse. In this passage, Ghazali reports—but does not endorse—their systematic analysis of key divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the pattern
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how each attribute is explained in terms of negation or relation. The pattern is consistent: each attribute is either a negation (salb) or a relation (idafa) or both.
Pay attention to the terms 'salb' and 'idafa'.
2
Identify the reduction for each attribute
Go through each divine name mentioned: First, Principle, Existent, Substance, Eternal, Everlasting, Necessary of Existence, Intellect, Intellector, Intelligible. For each, note whether it reduces to negation, relation, or both.
Use the commentary to clarify the reasoning.
3
Understand the threefold identity
Focus on the final part about 'Aql', 'Aqil', and 'Ma'qul'. The philosophers argue that in God, these three are identical because His essence is abstract, self-aware, and self-Intellecting.
This is a key point about divine simplicity.
4
Reflect on the difference from human intellect
Notice the closing remark: God's intellect is always actual, while human intellect is sometimes potential. This distinction reinforces the transcendence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقالوا : إذا قيل له : ( أول ) ، فهو إضافة إلى الموجودات بعده ، وإذا قيل ( مبدأ ) ، فهو إشارة إلى أن وجود غيره منه ، وهو سبب له ، فهو إضافة له إلى معلولاته ، وإذا قيل : (موجود) ، فمعناه : معلوم ، وإذا قيل : (جوهر ) فمعناه الوجود ، مسلوباً عنه الحلول في موضوع ، وهذا سلب ، وإذا قيل : ( قديم ) ، فمعناه سلب العدم عنه أولاً ، وإذا قيل : ( باق ) ، فمعناه سلب العدم عند آخراً ، فيرجع حاصل القديم والباقى ، إلى أن الوجود ليس مسبوقاً بعدم ، ولا ملحوقاً بعدم وإذا قيل : ( واجب الوجود ) ، فمعناه أنه لا علة له ، وهو علة لغيره ، فيكون جمعاً بين السلب والإضافة ، إذ نفى علة له ، سلب ؛ وجعله علة لغيره ، إضافة ، وإذا قيل ( عقل ) ، فمعناه موجود برىء من المادة ، وكل موجود هذه صفته ، فهو عقل ، أي يعقل ويشعر بها ، ويعقل غيره ، وذات الله تعالى هذه صفتها ، أى هو برىء عن المادة ، فإذن هو عقل ، وهما عبارتان (1) عن معنى واحد ، وإذا قيل : ( عاقل ) ، فمعناه أن ذاته الذي هو عقل ، له معقول ، هو ذاته ، فإنه يشعر بنفسه ، ويعقل نفسه ، فذاته معقول ، وذاته عاقل ، وذاته عقل ، والكل واحد ، إذ هو ( معقول ) ، من حيث إنه ماهية مجردة عن غير مستورة عن ذاته ، الذي هو عقل ، بمعنى أنه ماهية مجردة عن المادة ، لا يكون شيء مستوراً عنه ، ولما عقل نفسه ، كان عاقلا ، ولما كان نفسه معقولاً لنفسه ، كان معقولاً ، ولما كان عقله بذاته ، لا بزائد على ذاته ، كان عقلاً ، ولا يبعد أن يتحد العاقل (2) والمعقول ، فإن العاقل إذا عقل كونه عاقلا عقله : « كونه عاقلاً فيكون العاقل والمعقول واحداً بوجه وإن كان عقلنا يفارق عقل الأول ، فإن ما للأول بالفعل أبداً ، وما لنا يكون بالقوة تارة ، وبالفعل تارة أخرى .
They said: When He is called 'First', it is a relation to the existents after Him. And when He is called 'Principle', it is an indication that the existence of other than Him is from Him, and He is a cause for it, so it is a relation to His effects. And when He is called 'Existent', its meaning is 'known'. And when He is called 'Substance', its meaning is existence, with the negation of inherence in a subject, and this is a negation. And when He is called 'Eternal' (Qadim), its meaning is the negation of non-existence from Him at the beginning. And when He is called 'Everlasting' (Baqi), its meaning is the negation of non-existence from Him at the end. So the sum of 'Eternal' and 'Everlasting' reduces to that existence is not preceded by non-existence nor succeeded by non-existence. And when He is called 'Necessary of Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud), its meaning is that He has no cause, and He is a cause for other than Him. Thus it is a combination of negation and relation: for negating a cause for Him is a negation, and making Him a cause for other than Him is a relation. And when He is called 'Intellect' ('Aql), its meaning is an existent free from matter. And every existent with this description is an intellect, i.e., it intellects and is aware of itself, and intellects other than itself. And the essence of God Most High has this description, i.e., He is free from matter. Therefore He is an intellect. And the two expressions ('He intellects Himself' and 'He intellects other than Himself') refer to a single meaning. And when He is called 'Intellector' ('Aqil), its meaning is that His essence, which is intellect, has an intelligible, which is His essence. For He is aware of Himself and intellects Himself. So His essence is intelligible, His essence is intellector, and His essence is intellect—all is one. For He is 'intelligible' insofar as He is an essence abstracted from other, not veiled from His essence, which is intellect, meaning that He is an essence abstracted from matter, so nothing is veiled from Him. And since He intellects Himself, He is an intellector. And since His self is intelligible to itself, He is intelligible. And since His intellection is by His essence, not by something additional to His essence, He is intellect. And it is not improbable that the intellector and the intelligible are one. For when the intellector intellects its being an intellector, its intellection is 'its being an intellector'; thus the intellector and the intelligible are one in a certain respect, even though our intellect differs from the First Intellect, for what belongs to the First is always actual, while what belongs to us is sometimes potential and sometimes actual.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage reports the philosophers' method of reducing all essential divine attributes to either a negation (salb) or a relation (idafa). The author (Ghazali) is presenting their view, not endorsing it. He goes through each attribute: 'First' is a relation to what comes after; 'Principle' is a relation to effects; 'Existent' reduces to 'known'; 'Substance' reduces to existence with negation of inherence; 'Eternal' and 'Everlasting' reduce to the double negation of non-existence; 'Necessary of Existence' combines negation (no cause) and relation (cause of others). Then he explains how 'Intellect', 'Intellector', and 'Intelligible' all refer to one reality in God because His essence is abstract from matter, self-aware, and His intellection is identical to His essence. The passage closes by noting that our intellect differs from God's since ours is sometimes potential, while His is always actual.

Key Terms
سلب (salb)
إضافة (idafa)
قديم (qadim)
باق (baqi)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
عقل ('aql)
عاقل ('aqil)
معقول (ma'qul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers are denying the existence of divine attributes altogether.
Correction: They are not denying attributes but reinterpreting them as either negations of imperfections or relations to creation, thus preserving God's simplicity without eliminating meaningful description.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as reported here, to what do the attributes 'Eternal' (Qadim) and 'Everlasting' (Baqi) reduce?
A) Two distinct positive attributes
B) The negation of non-existence at the beginning and at the end
C) A relation to creation
D) The attribute of existence

Key Takeaway
The philosophers systematically reduce divine attributes to negations and relations to defend God's simplicity while maintaining meaningful discourse about His nature.

The Philosophers' Explanation of Creator, Agent, and Maker
Ghazali presents the philosophers' reduction of active attributes to knowledge and necessary emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers reinterpret the divine attributes of action (Creator, Agent, Maker) as expressions of God's eternal knowledge causing the necessary emanation of the universe.

Core Idea
God's action is not separate from His knowledge; His knowing Himself as the principle of all is the cause of creation.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' account of what it means to say that God is 'Creator' or 'Agent'. They argue that these attributes do not indicate any addition to God's essence, but rather describe the relationship between His eternal knowledge and the necessary emanation of the cosmos. The passage uses analogies and a detailed comparison to clarify how this differs from natural causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the core claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the philosophers are defining the meaning of terms like 'Creator' and 'Agent'.
The key phrase is that God's existence is a 'noble existence from which the existence of the whole emanates necessarily'.
2
Understand the analogy with sun and fire
The philosophers liken emanation to light from the sun and heat from fire, but point out a crucial difference: God is aware of the emanation.
This differentiates it from natural, unconscious causation.
3
Examine the shadow analogy
The passage compares God to a person who deliberately casts a shadow to benefit a sick person. This illustrates that God intends His creation and is pleased with it.
Note the distinction between the agent (the person) and the body; in God, the agent is His knowledge itself.
4
Grasp the reduction of action to knowledge
The conclusion is that God's being an agent is not additional to His being knower; His knowledge of Himself as the principle is the cause of emanation.
This is the core philosophical reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : ( خالق ) ، ( وفاعل ) ، ( وبارئ ) ، وسائر صفات الفعل ، فمعناه أن وجوده وجود شريف ، يفيض عنه وجود الكل فيضاناً لازماً ، وأن وجود غيره حاصل منه وتابع لوجوده كما يتبع النور الشمس والأسخان النار ، ولا تشبه نسبة العالم إليه ، نسبة النور إلى الشمس ، إلا فى كونه معلولاً فقط ، وإلا فليس هو كذلك ، فإن الشمس لا تشعر بفيضان النور عنها ، ولا النار بفيضان الإسخان ، فهو طبع محض ، بل الأول عالم بذاته ، وأن ذاته مبدأ لوجود غيره ، ففيضان ما يفيض عنه معلوم له ، فليس به غفلة عما يصدر عنه ، ولا هو أيضاً كالواحد منا ، إذا وقف بين مريض وبين الشمس ، فاندفع حر الشمس عن المريض بسببه لا باختياره ، وأنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره أى الظل - ؛ وإن كان الواقف أيضاً مريداً لوقوع الظل فلا يشبهه أيضاً فإن المظل الفاعل للظل شخصه وجسمه ، والعالم الراضى بوقوع الظل نفسه لا جسمه ، وفى حق الأول ليس كذلك ، فإن الفاعل منه هو العالم ، وهو الراضى - أى أنه غير كاره له - فإنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره . بل لو أمكن أن يفرض كون الجسم المظل نفسه ، هو العالم نفسه بوقوع الظل ، وهو الراضى ، لم يكن أيضاً مساوياً للأول ، فإن الأول هو العالم وهو الفاعل وعلمه هو مبدأ فعله ، فإن علمه بنفسه في كونه مبدأ للكل ، علة فيضان الكل ، فإن النظام الموجود تبع للنظام المعقول ، بمعنى أنه واقع به ، فكونه فاعلاً غير زائد على كونه عالماً بالكل ، إذ علمه بالكل علة فيضان الكل عنه ، وكونه عالماً بالكل لا يزيد على علمه بذاته ، فإنه لا يعلم ذاته ، ما لم يعلم أنه مبدأ للكل ، فيكون المعلوم بالقصد الأول ذاته ، ويكون الكل معلوماً عنده بالقصد الثانى ، فهذا معنى كونه فاعلاً ، وإذا قيل : ( قادر ) ، لم يعن به إلا كونه فاعلا على الوجه الذى قررناه ، وهو أن وجوده وجود تفيض عنه المقدورات ، التي بفيضانها ينتظم الترتيب فى الكل على أبلغ وجوه الإمكان ، فى الكمال والحسن . وإذا قيل : ( مريد ) ، لم يعن به إلا أن ما يفيض عنه ، ليس هو غافلا عنه ، وليس كارهاً له ، بل هو عالم بأن كماله في فيضان الكل عنه ، فيحسن بهذا المعنى أن يقال : هو راض ، وجاز أن يقال للراضى :
And when He is called 'Creator' (Khalik), 'Agent' (Fa'il), 'Maker' (Bari'), and the rest of the attributes of action, its meaning is that His existence is a noble existence from which the existence of the whole emanates necessarily, and that the existence of other than Him arises from Him and follows His existence as light follows the sun and heat follows fire. Yet the relation of the world to Him is not like the relation of light to the sun except in being an effect only; otherwise it is not that, for the sun is not aware of the emanation of light from it, nor is fire aware of the emanation of heat—it is mere nature. Rather, the First is knowing of His essence, and His essence is the principle of the existence of other than Him. So the emanation of what emanates from Him is known to Him. Therefore He is not heedless of what proceeds from Him. Nor is He like one of us when he stands between a sick person and the sun, and the sun's heat is repelled from the sick person because of him, not by his choice—and he knows that his perfection is in the emanation of other from him, i.e., the shadow. And even if the standing person also wills the occurrence of the shadow, it does not resemble Him either, for the shadow-caster, the agent of the shadow, is his person and body, while the knower who is pleased with the occurrence of the shadow is himself, not his body. In the case of the First, it is not so. For the agent from Him is the knower, and He is the one pleased—i.e., He is not averse to it—for He knows that His perfection is in the emanation of other from Him. Even if it were possible to suppose that the body that casts the shadow is itself the knower of the occurrence of the shadow and the one pleased, it would still not be equal to the First, for the First is the knower and the agent, and His knowledge is the principle of His action. For His knowledge of Himself, in being the principle of the whole, is the cause of the emanation of the whole. For the existing order follows the intellectual order, meaning it occurs through it. Thus His being an agent is not additional to His being knower of the whole, since His knowledge of the whole is the cause of the emanation of the whole from Him. And His being knower of the whole is not additional to His knowledge of Himself, for He does not know Himself unless He knows that He is the principle of the whole. So the known by primary intention is His essence, and the whole is known to Him by secondary intention. This is the meaning of His being an agent. And when He is called 'Powerful' (Qadir), nothing is meant by it except that He is an agent in the manner we have explained, namely that His existence is an existence from which the things over which He has power (al-maqdurat) emanate, by whose emanation the order in the whole is arranged in the most perfect possible way in terms of perfection and beauty. And when He is called 'Willing' (Murid), nothing is meant by it except that what emanates from Him is not such that He is heedless of it, nor averse to it, but rather He knows that His perfection is in the emanation of the whole from Him. So in this sense it is fitting to say He is 'well-pleased' (Radi), and it is permissible to say of the well-pleased (al-radi):
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage continues the presentation of the philosophers' reduction of divine attributes, now focusing on the attributes of action such as Creator, Agent, Maker, Powerful, and Willing. The key claim is that God's action is identical to His knowledge: He knows Himself as the principle of the whole, and that knowledge is the cause of the emanation of the universe. The emanation is necessary and eternal, not a free choice in the sense of deliberating between alternatives. The philosophers illustrate this with analogies: light from the sun and heat from fire, but note that unlike the sun, God is aware of His act and He intends it because it is part of His perfection. The attribute 'Powerful' is then explained as being an agent in this specific sense, and 'Willing' is explained as not being heedless or averse, but rather being pleased because it is His perfection. The passage ends mid-sentence on the page, discussing that 'well-pleased' can be said of Him.

Key Terms
خالق (khalik)
فاعل (fa'il)
بارئ (bari')
فيضان (fayadan)
علة ('illa)
علم ('ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view makes God a necessary cause like nature, with no awareness or will.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that God is aware of the emanation and that it is known to Him. Unlike natural causes, He is not heedless.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what distinguishes God's emanation from the sun's emanation of light?
A) The sun's emanation is necessary, God's is free
B) God is aware of the emanation, the sun is not
C) The sun's emanation is eternal, God's is not
D) God's emanation is by choice, the sun's is by nature

Key Takeaway
For the philosophers, being an agent means that God's knowledge of Himself as the principle of all causes the universe to emanate necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is the very cause of your existence and every moment of your life, then nothing in your day is outside His awareness. What thought, word, or action from yesterday would you be ashamed to have known by the One whose knowledge is the principle of all? Will you bring that before Him in repentance today?
?
The philosophers say that God's perfection lies in emanating existence to others. You are created in His image—not in essence, but in capacity for giving. In what specific area of your life are you hoarding your gifts (time, mercy, knowledge) rather than letting them emanate? Name one concrete way you will give of yourself today, even if no one notices.
?
The passage insists that God is not heedless of what proceeds from Him. Yet you often live as if your small sins are inconsequential to the universe. If the One who sustains the heavens is fully aware of your every thought, how must you change your inner dialogue to align with this reality? What intention will you set the moment you wake tomorrow?

The Meanings of 'Powerful' and 'Willing' in the Philosophers' System
Ghazali completes the philosophers' reduction by defining 'Powerful' and 'Willing' within the same knowledge-based framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers define 'Powerful' (Qadir) and 'Willing' (Murid) purely in terms of knowledge-based necessary emanation, rejecting any separate attribute.

Core Idea
God's power and will are just His agency, which is His knowledge causing emanation perfectly.

Having established that being an agent is identical to being knower, the philosophers now explain what it means to say that God is 'Powerful' and 'Willing'. They define these attributes in the same reductive framework: power is agency as already described, and will is the absence of heedlessness and aversion, rooted in God's knowledge that emanation is His perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation. See how 'Powerful' is defined as being an agent in the manner already described, and 'Willing' is defined as not being heedless or averse.
The definitions are very concise.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Willing' as 'pleased'
The passage says that in this sense it is fitting to say God is 'well-pleased' (Radi). This is a reinterpretation of will as knowledge-based satisfaction.
Will is not a separate faculty but a consequence of knowledge.
3
Connect back to the earlier reduction
Recall that in the previous lesson, agency was reduced to knowledge of self as principle. Here, power and will are not additional; they are just further descriptions of that same reality.

وإذا قيل : ( خالق ) ، ( وفاعل ) ، ( وبارئ ) ، وسائر صفات الفعل ، فمعناه أن وجوده وجود شريف ، يفيض عنه وجود الكل فيضاناً لازماً ، وأن وجود غيره حاصل منه وتابع لوجوده كما يتبع النور الشمس والأسخان النار ، ولا تشبه نسبة العالم إليه ، نسبة النور إلى الشمس ، إلا فى كونه معلولاً فقط ، وإلا فليس هو كذلك ، فإن الشمس لا تشعر بفيضان النور عنها ، ولا النار بفيضان الإسخان ، فهو طبع محض ، بل الأول عالم بذاته ، وأن ذاته مبدأ لوجود غيره ، ففيضان ما يفيض عنه معلوم له ، فليس به غفلة عما يصدر عنه ، ولا هو أيضاً كالواحد منا ، إذا وقف بين مريض وبين الشمس ، فاندفع حر الشمس عن المريض بسببه لا باختياره ، وأنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره أى الظل - ؛ وإن كان الواقف أيضاً مريداً لوقوع الظل فلا يشبهه أيضاً فإن المظل الفاعل للظل شخصه وجسمه ، والعالم الراضى بوقوع الظل نفسه لا جسمه ، وفى حق الأول ليس كذلك ، فإن الفاعل منه هو العالم ، وهو الراضى - أى أنه غير كاره له - فإنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره . بل لو أمكن أن يفرض كون الجسم المظل نفسه ، هو العالم نفسه بوقوع الظل ، وهو الراضى ، لم يكن أيضاً مساوياً للأول ، فإن الأول هو العالم وهو الفاعل وعلمه هو مبدأ فعله ، فإن علمه بنفسه في كونه مبدأ للكل ، علة فيضان الكل ، فإن النظام الموجود تبع للنظام المعقول ، بمعنى أنه واقع به ، فكونه فاعلاً غير زائد على كونه عالماً بالكل ، إذ علمه بالكل علة فيضان الكل عنه ، وكونه عالماً بالكل لا يزيد على علمه بذاته ، فإنه لا يعلم ذاته ، ما لم يعلم أنه مبدأ للكل ، فيكون المعلوم بالقصد الأول ذاته ، ويكون الكل معلوماً عنده بالقصد الثانى ، فهذا معنى كونه فاعلاً ، وإذا قيل : ( قادر ) ، لم يعن به إلا كونه فاعلا على الوجه الذى قررناه ، وهو أن وجوده وجود تفيض عنه المقدورات ، التي بفيضانها ينتظم الترتيب فى الكل على أبلغ وجوه الإمكان ، فى الكمال والحسن . وإذا قيل : ( مريد ) ، لم يعن به إلا أن ما يفيض عنه ، ليس هو غافلا عنه ، وليس كارهاً له ، بل هو عالم بأن كماله في فيضان الكل عنه ، فيحسن بهذا المعنى أن يقال : هو راض ، وجاز أن يقال للراضى :
And when He is called 'Creator' (Khalik), 'Agent' (Fa'il), 'Maker' (Bari'), and the rest of the attributes of action, its meaning is that His existence is a noble existence from which the existence of the whole emanates necessarily, and that the existence of other than Him arises from Him and follows His existence as light follows the sun and heat follows fire. Yet the relation of the world to Him is not like the relation of light to the sun except in being an effect only; otherwise it is not that, for the sun is not aware of the emanation of light from it, nor is fire aware of the emanation of heat—it is mere nature. Rather, the First is knowing of His essence, and His essence is the principle of the existence of other than Him. So the emanation of what emanates from Him is known to Him. Therefore He is not heedless of what proceeds from Him. Nor is He like one of us when he stands between a sick person and the sun, and the sun's heat is repelled from the sick person because of him, not by his choice—and he knows that his perfection is in the emanation of other from him, i.e., the shadow. And even if the standing person also wills the occurrence of the shadow, it does not resemble Him either, for the shadow-caster, the agent of the shadow, is his person and body, while the knower who is pleased with the occurrence of the shadow is himself, not his body. In the case of the First, it is not so. For the agent from Him is the knower, and He is the one pleased—i.e., He is not averse to it—for He knows that His perfection is in the emanation of other from Him. Even if it were possible to suppose that the body that casts the shadow is itself the knower of the occurrence of the shadow and the one pleased, it would still not be equal to the First, for the First is the knower and the agent, and His knowledge is the principle of His action. For His knowledge of Himself, in being the principle of the whole, is the cause of the emanation of the whole. For the existing order follows the intellectual order, meaning it occurs through it. Thus His being an agent is not additional to His being knower of the whole, since His knowledge of the whole is the cause of the emanation of the whole from Him. And His being knower of the whole is not additional to His knowledge of Himself, for He does not know Himself unless He knows that He is the principle of the whole. So the known by primary intention is His essence, and the whole is known to Him by secondary intention. This is the meaning of His being an agent. And when He is called 'Powerful' (Qadir), nothing is meant by it except that He is an agent in the manner we have explained, namely that His existence is an existence from which the things over which He has power (al-maqdurat) emanate, by whose emanation the order in the whole is arranged in the most perfect possible way in terms of perfection and beauty. And when He is called 'Willing' (Murid), nothing is meant by it except that what emanates from Him is not such that He is heedless of it, nor averse to it, but rather He knows that His perfection is in the emanation of the whole from Him. So in this sense it is fitting to say He is 'well-pleased' (Radi), and it is permissible to say of the well-pleased (al-radi):
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage continues the presentation of the philosophers' reduction of divine attributes, now focusing on the attributes of action such as Creator, Agent, Maker, Powerful, and Willing. The key claim is that God's action is identical to His knowledge: He knows Himself as the principle of the whole, and that knowledge is the cause of the emanation of the universe. The emanation is necessary and eternal, not a free choice in the sense of deliberating between alternatives. The philosophers illustrate this with analogies: light from the sun and heat from fire, but note that unlike the sun, God is aware of His act and He intends it because it is part of His perfection. The attribute 'Powerful' is then explained as being an agent in this specific sense, and 'Willing' is explained as not being heedless or averse, but rather being pleased because it is His perfection. The passage ends mid-sentence on the page, discussing that 'well-pleased' can be said of Him.

Key Terms
قادر (qadir)
مريد (murid)
رضا (rida)
فيضان (fayadan)
حسن (husn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers completely deny that God has will or power, reducing everything to knowledge.
Correction: They do not deny these terms; they reinterpret them. Power is the emanation itself, and will is the absence of heedlessness and aversion, grounded in knowledge of perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'Willing' (Murid) as applied to God?
A) God has a separate faculty of volition
B) God is not heedless and not averse to the emanation
C) God chooses among alternatives
D) God desires the creation for a purpose

Key Takeaway
For the philosophers, 'Powerful' means God's existence perfectly emanates the universe, and 'Willing' means He is not heedless or averse, knowing that emanation is His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers say God's power is simply the necessary emanation of the best possible order. Do you truly believe that everything that happens in your life—even the trials—is part of that perfect order? Or do you sometimes wish God had arranged things differently? How does this challenge your gratitude and submission?
?
If God's 'will' means He is never heedless of what emanates from Him, how should that shape your trust in His awareness of every detail of your life? Are you living with the confidence that He is fully present to your situation, or do you act as if He is distant?
?
The passage says God's perfection is in the emanation of the whole. Reflect: Do you measure your own worth by what you produce or emanate? How can you shift from seeking perfection in your own accomplishments to recognizing that your true perfection lies in submitting to God's perfect order?

The Meaning of Bari' and Fa'il for God
Ghazali explains the philosophical meaning of Creator and Agent for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defines the philosophical terms 'Bari'' (Creator) and 'Fa'il' (Agent) for God, distinguishing divine creative action from natural causal processes.

Core Idea
God's creation is a knowing, willing emanation, not a blind natural necessity.

Ghazali delves into the philosophical understanding of God as Creator (Bari') and Agent (Fa'il). He uses the analogy of light from the sun and heat from fire, but crucially points out where the analogy fails: natural emanations are unconscious, while God's creative act is accompanied by knowledge and approval. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali begins by defining the terms then immediately introduces an analogy.
2
Identify the key analogy and its limit
The analogy is light from the sun and heat from fire. Note how Ghazali first says the world's relation to God is like an effect, but then corrects the analogy because the sun and fire are unaware. Write down why this matters.
3
Understand the two refinements
Ghazali refines the analogy in two ways: (1) God is knowing of Himself and of the emanation, so it is not blind; (2) God is not like a person accidentally causing a shadow, because the agent is the knower, not the body. Trace these points in the text.
The first refinement addresses knowledge, the second addresses will and agency.
4
Synthesize the meaning of Bari'
Combine the ideas: 'Bari'' means God is the knowing, approving source from whom all existence flows necessarily. Write one sentence that captures this definition in your own words.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وبارئ ) ، وسائر صفات الفعل ، فمعناه أن وجوده وجود شريف ، يفيض عنه وجود الكل فيضاناً لازماً ، وأن وجود غيره حاصل منه وتابع لوجوده كما يتبع النور الشمس والأسخان النار ، ولا تشبه نسبة العالم إليه ، نسبة النور إلى الشمس ، إلا فى كونه معلولاً فقط ، وإلا فليس هو كذلك ، فإن الشمس لا تشعر بفيضان النور عنها ، ولا النار بفيضان الإسخان ، فهو طبع محض ، بل الأول عالم بذاته ، وأن ذاته مبدأ لوجود غيره ، ففيضان ما يفيض عنه معلوم له ، فليس به غفلة عما يصدر عنه ، ولا هو أيضاً كالواحد منا ، إذا وقف بين مريض وبين الشمس ، فاندفع حر الشمس عن المريض بسببه لا باختياره ، وأنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره أى الظل - ؛ وإن كان الواقف أيضاً مريداً لوقوع الظل فلا يشبهه أيضاً فإن المظل الفاعل للظل شخصه وجسمه ، والعالم الراضى بوقوع الظل نفسه لا جسمه ، وفى حق الأول ليس كذلك ، فإن الفاعل منه هو العالم ، وهو الراضى - أى أنه غير كاره له - فإنه عالم بأن كماله في أن يفيض عنه غيره
As for (the Creator) and the rest of the attributes of action, its meaning is that His existence is a noble existence from which the existence of everything emanates necessarily; and that the existence of other than Him derives from Him and is consequent upon His existence, just as light follows the sun and heat follows fire. Yet the relationship of the world to Him does not resemble the relationship of light to the sun, except in being an effect only; otherwise it is not so, because the sun does not perceive the emanation of light from it, nor does the fire perceive the emanation of heat – that is mere nature. Rather, the First is knowing of His Essence, and that His Essence is the principle of the existence of other than Him. So the emanation of what emanates from Him is known to Him; He is not heedless of what proceeds from Him. Nor is He like one of us who stands between a sick person and the sun, where the sun’s heat is repelled from the sick person by his [the standing person's] means without his choice – and he knows that his perfection lies in the emanation of something else from him, i.e., the shadow. Even if the standing person also wills the occurrence of the shadow, he still does not resemble Him, because the one who makes the shadow, the agent of the shadow, is his person and body, while the one knowing and approving of the shadow’s occurrence is his self, not his body. In the case of the First, it is not so. For the agent from Him is the knower, and He is the approver – that is, He is not averse to it – because He knows that His perfection lies in the emanation of other than Him from Him.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of 'Bari'' (Creator) and other attributes of action as applied to God according to the philosophers. Ghazali explains that God's existence is such that all other existence emanates from Him necessarily, like light from the sun or heat from fire. However, the analogy is incomplete because the sun and fire are unaware of their emanations – they act by mere nature. In contrast, the First is knowing of Himself and of the fact that He is the source of all else; thus His emanation is not blind but known. Ghazali then refines further: the First is not like a person who accidentally blocks the sun, producing a shadow without intending it, because that person's action is not from his will but from his body. Even if the person wills the shadow, the agent producing the shadow is the body, not the self. In God, the agent is precisely the knower and the approver – He knows that His perfection lies in the emanation of other beings, so He is not averse to it. Thus 'Bari'' means that God is the knowing, approving source of all existence.

Key Terms
بارئ
فاعل
فيضان
عالم بذاته
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's act of creation is like the sun emitting light – a necessary, unconscious flow.
Correction: The passage explains that while the effect is necessary, God is aware of and approves the emanation, unlike the sun which has no knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's exposition of the philosophers, how does God's act of creation differ from the sun's emission of light?
A) The sun acts by knowledge, God by nature.
B) God is aware of His emanation, while the sun is not.
C) The sun's light is not an emanation but a creation.
D) There is no difference; both are necessary.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of being Creator means He is the knowing, approving source from whom all existence emanates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence is a 'noble existence' from which everything emanates with knowledge and will, why do you often live as though your existence is accidental or meaningless? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed you are a known and willed creation?
?
Ghazali says God knows that His perfection lies in the emanation of other beings. In contrast, do you seek your own perfection in yourself alone, or do you recognize that your perfection is found in being a channel of His emanation—in giving, creating, and serving others? Where are you holding back, as if your perfection is something you hoard?
?
The sun and fire emanate without awareness, but God emanates with full knowledge and approval. Are there areas of your life where you act like the sun—going through motions unconsciously, without attention or intention? What is one habit you perform 'naturally' that you now commit to doing with full knowledge and will, as an act of worship?

The Identity of Power and Will with Knowledge
Ghazali explains that Qadir and Murid reduce to knowledge of the emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues that the divine attributes of power and will are identical to knowledge and therefore to the essence itself.

Core Idea
Power and will are not separate attributes – they are knowledge itself.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents a key argument from the philosophers that the attributes of power (Qadir) and will (Murid) are not distinct from God's knowledge. He explains that God's agency is simply His knowledge of Himself as the source of all things. This lays the foundation for understanding the unity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the flow of the argument from the shadow analogy to the conclusion.
The passage begins mid-sentence from a previous context.
2
Identify the chain of identities
Trace the argument: how does Ghazali equate power with knowledge? Look for the reasoning: 'will is nothing other than power, nor power other than knowledge, nor knowledge other than the essence.'
The key is that God's knowledge of Himself as the cause of everything is itself the cause of emanation.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'agent' and 'willing'
Consider what it means for God to be an agent (fāʿil) and willing (murīd) in this framework. How does Ghazali define these terms without adding anything to knowledge?
The definition of agent is 'His existence is an existence from which things emanate in the best order.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لو أمكن أن يفرض كون الجسم المظل نفسه ، هو العالم نفسه بوقوع الظل ، وهو الراضى ، لم يكن أيضاً مساوياً للأول ، فإن الأول هو العالم وهو الفاعل وعلمه هو مبدأ فعله ، فإن علمه بنفسه في كونه مبدأ للكل ، علة فيضان الكل ، فإن النظام الموجود تبع للنظام المعقول ، بمعنى أنه واقع به ، فكونه فاعلاً غير زائد على كونه عالماً بالكل ، إذ علمه بالكل علة فيضان الكل عنه ، وكونه عالماً بالكل لا يزيد على علمه بذاته ، فإنه لا يعلم ذاته ، ما لم يعلم أنه مبدأ للكل ، فيكون المعلوم بالقصد الأول ذاته ، ويكون الكل معلوماً عنده بالقصد الثانى ، فهذا معنى كونه فاعلاً ، وإذا قيل : ( قادر ) ، لم يعن به إلا كونه فاعلا على الوجه الذى قررناه ، وهو أن وجوده وجود تفيض عنه المقدورات ، التي بفيضانها ينتظم الترتيب فى الكل على أبلغ وجوه الإمكان ، فى الكمال والحسن . وإذا قيل : ( مريد ) ، لم يعن به إلا أن ما يفيض عنه ، غافلا عنه ، وليس كارهاً له ، بل هو عالم بأن كماله في فيضان الكل عنه ، فيحسن بهذا المعنى أن يقال : هو راض ، وجاز أن يقال للراضى : إنه مريد ، فلا تكون الإرادة إلا عين القدرة ، ولا القدرة إلا عين العلم ولا العلم إلا عين الذات ، فالكل إذن يرجع إلى عين الذات
Even if one could suppose that the very body that produces the shadow were itself the knower of the shadow's occurrence and the approver, it would still not equal the First. For the First is the knower and the agent, and His knowledge is the principle of His action. Indeed, His knowledge of Himself as the principle of everything is the cause of the emanation of everything. For the existing order follows the intelligible order, meaning it occurs through it. Thus His being an agent is not additional to His being knower of everything, since His knowledge of everything is the cause of the emanation of everything from Him, and His being knower of everything does not add to His knowledge of His Essence, because He does not know His Essence except by knowing that He is the principle of everything. So the known in the first intention is His Essence, and everything is known to Him in the second intention. This is the meaning of His being an agent. And when it is said (Powerful), nothing is meant except that He is an agent in the manner we have explained, namely that His existence is an existence from which the things He has power over emanate, and by their emanation the order in everything is arranged in the most perfect and beautiful manner possible. And when it is said (Willing), nothing is meant except that what emanates from Him is not from Him while He is heedless of it nor averse to it; rather, He knows that His perfection lies in the emanation of everything from Him. So in this sense it is correct to say He is approving, and it is permissible to call the approver willing. Thus will is nothing other than power itself, nor power other than knowledge itself, nor knowledge other than the Essence itself. So all then reduces to the Essence itself.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage explains the attributes of power (Qadir) and will (Murid) as understood by the philosophers, showing their identity with knowledge and essence. Ghazali begins by refining a hypothetical: even if the body that produced the shadow were also the knower and approver, it would still not equal God. For God's knowledge of Himself as the source is the very cause of the emanation; His agency is not a separate addition to His knowledge. The existing order of the world flows from the intelligible order in His mind. Thus 'powerful' means that His existence is such that things emanate from Him in the best possible order, and 'willing' means that He is not heedless or averse, but knows that His perfection lies in that emanation. Consequently, will is identical to power, power to knowledge, and knowledge to the essence. All attributes reduce to the single Essence.

Key Terms
قادر
مريد
فاعل
علم
قدرة
إرادة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe that God has multiple distinct attributes (power, will, knowledge) that are separate realities.
Correction: According to this passage, they hold that power, will, and knowledge are identical to each other and to the divine essence. The terms are different aspects of the same reality.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between God's will and His knowledge?
A) Will is additional to knowledge.
B) Will is identical to knowledge.
C) Will is a property of power, not knowledge.
D) Will is the cause of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali explains that power, will, and knowledge are identical and all reduce to the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power and will are not separate from His knowledge, then every action you perform—good or bad—was known and willed by God in the most perfect way possible. How does this truth challenge your tendency to complain about your circumstances or to take credit for your successes? Will you commit to seeing every moment as flowing from God's perfect knowledge, even the painful ones?
?
The passage says God's perfection lies in the emanation of everything from Him. That includes your every sin, your every failure. Does believing that God's will is identical to His knowledge make you feel less responsible for your actions? Or does it deepen your humility and reliance on His mercy? Examine your heart: do you use divine predestination as an excuse or as a reason to repent more sincerely?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Powerful,' do you mentally picture a power that could have acted differently? This lesson says His power is simply His knowledge itself—necessary and unchanging. Does that make His commands seem more or less urgent to you? If His will is necessary, how does that affect your daily struggle to obey Him? Will you start seeing obedience not as pleasing a capricious will, but as aligning with the very order of reality that flows from His essence?

Divine Attributes

Divine Knowledge, Life, and Generosity
Ghazali contrasts human and divine knowledge, then defines Hayy and Jawad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali distinguishes divine knowledge from human knowledge and why divine attributes like Life and Generosity are identical to God's essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is creative, not receptive; His attributes are one with His essence.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a central issue in Islamic theology: how to speak of God's attributes without implying composition or dependence. He contrasts our limited, derived knowledge with God's self-sufficient, creative knowledge. Then he applies this to the attributes of Life (Hayy) and Generosity (Jawad), showing they are not separate faculties but aspects of the same simple essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the contrast between human and divine knowledge.
The Arabic has a possible scribal repetition around 'للكان' – just note it.
2
Identify the two kinds of human knowledge
Ghazali distinguishes knowledge derived from things (like our knowledge of heaven) from invented knowledge (like a picture we conceive and then produce). Which one applies to God?
God's knowledge is the second kind: creative.
3
Understand the chain of human action
Ghazali explains that due to our bodily composition, we need a chain: conception, will, desire, muscle movement, limbs, tools, material. This shows our knowledge is not power or will.
In God, there is no composition, so knowledge, power, will are one.
4
Define 'Living' (Hayy) and 'Generous' (Jawad)
Living means He is a knower from whose knowledge existence emanates. Generous means He gives without benefit to Himself, and the gift benefits the recipient.
These definitions emphasize God's independence and generosity.
5
Reflect on the unity of divine attributes
Contrast with human life, which requires two forces (perception and action). God's life is identical to His essence, not composed.
This upholds tawhid and avoids multiplicity in God.

وهذا لأن علمه بالأشياء ، ليس مأخوذاً من الأشياء ، وإلا لكان مستفيداً وصفاً أو كمالا من غيره ، وهو محال في واجب الوجود ، ولكن علمنا على قسمين ، ( علم ) شيء حصل من صورة ذلك الشيء ، كعلمنا بصورة السماء والأرض ، و ( علم ) اخترعناه ، كشيء لم نشاهد صورته ، ولكن صورناه في أنفسنا ، ثم أحدثناه ، فيكون وجود الصورة مستفاداً من العلم ، لا العلم من الوجود ، وعلم الأول بحسب القسم الثانى ، فإن تمثل النظام في ذاته ، سبب لفيضان النظام عن نعم لو كان مجرد حضور صورة نقش أو كتابة خط في نفوسنا ، كافياً في حدوث تلك الصورة ، لكان ، لكان العلم بعينه منا ، هو القدرة بعينها ، والإرادة بعينها ولكنا لقصورنا ليس يكفى تصورنا ، لإيجاد الصورة ، بل نحتاج ذلك إلى إرادة متجددة ، تنبعث من قوة شوقية ، ليتحرك منهما معاً القوة المحركة للعضل والأعصاب ، فى الأعضاء الآلية ، فيتحرك بحركة العضل والأعصاب ، اليد أو غيرها ، ويتحرك بحركتها ، القلم أو آلة أخرى خارجة ، وتتحرك المادة بحركة القلم ، كالمداد أو غيره ، ثم تحصل الصورة المتصورة في نفوسنا ، فلذلك لم يكن نفس وجود هذه الصورة في نفوسنا ، قدرة ولا إرادة ، بل كانت القدرة فينا عن المبدأ المحرك للعضل ، وهذه الصورة محركة لذلك المحرك للعضل ، الذي هو مبدأ القدرة ، وليس كذلك في واجب الوجود ، فإنه ليس مركباً من أجسام تنبث القوى في أطرافها ، فكانت القدرة ، والإرادة ، والعلم ، والذات ، منه واحداً ، وإذا قيل له : ( حى ) ، لم يرد به إلا أنه عالم علماً ، يفيض عنه الوجود ، الذي فعلا له ، فإن الحى هو الدراك ، فيكون المراد به ذاته مع إضافة إلى الأفعال ، على الوجه الذي ذكرناه . لا كحياتنا ، فإنها لا تتم إلا بقوتين مختلفتين ، ينبعث عنهما الإدراك والفعل فحياته عين ذاته أيضاً ، وإذا قيل له : جواد ، أريد به أنه يفيض عنه الكل ، لا لغرض يرجع إليه ، والجود يتم بشيئين : أحدهما ، أن يكون للمنعم عليه فائدة فيما وهب له ، له ، فإن من يهب شيئاً من هو مستغن عنه ، لا يوصف بالجود
This is because His knowledge of things is not taken from things, otherwise He would be acquiring a description or perfection from another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Rather, our knowledge is of two kinds: (1) knowledge that comes from the form of that thing, like our knowledge of the form of the heaven and the earth; and (2) knowledge that we invent, like something we have not seen its form but we picture it in ourselves and then bring it about. In the latter, the existence of the form is derived from the knowledge, not knowledge from existence. And the knowledge of the First is of the second kind. For the representation of the order in His essence is the cause of the emanation of order from Him. For if the mere presence of a form of a picture or a line of writing in ourselves were sufficient for the occurrence of that picture, then that very knowledge from us would be power itself and will itself. But due to our deficiency, our conception is not sufficient to bring about the picture; rather we need a renewed will that arises from a desiring power, so that from both together the power that moves muscles and nerves in the instrumental organs moves, and by the movement of muscles and nerves the hand or other [limb] moves, and by its movement the pen or other external tool moves, and the material moves by the movement of the pen, like ink or other, and then the pictured form comes about in ourselves. Therefore, the mere existence of this form in ourselves is not power nor will; rather, power in us comes from the principle that moves the muscle, and this form moves that mover of the muscle which is the principle of power. This is not the case for the Necessary Existent, for He is not composed of bodies with forces spread throughout their parts. Thus power, will, knowledge, and essence are one from Him. And when He is called (Living), nothing is meant except that He is knowing with a knowledge from which existence emanates, which is action for Him. For 'the living' is the perceiver; so what is meant is His essence with an addition to actions in the manner we have mentioned – not like our life, for it is only completed by two different forces from which perception and action emanate. So His life is also identical to His essence. And when He is called (Generous), what is meant is that everything emanates from Him, not for a purpose that returns to Himself. Completeness of generosity requires two things: one, that the beneficiary derive benefit from what is given to him; for one who gives something to someone who is self-sufficient from it is not described as generous.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of divine knowledge in contrast to human knowledge, and then defines the attributes of Life (Hayy) and Generosity (Jawad). Ghazali explains that God's knowledge is not derived from things, otherwise He would depend on them. Human knowledge has two types: one derived from the forms of existing things (like our knowledge of heaven and earth), and another invented by us (like a picture we conceive and then produce). God's knowledge is of the second type: His representation of the cosmic order in His essence is the cause of that order's emanation. If our mere conception were sufficient to produce things, our knowledge would be identical to power and will, but due to our composition and need for bodily instruments, we require a chain of movements. In God, there is no such composition; hence His knowledge, power, will, and essence are one. Then Ghazali defines 'Living': it means He is a knower from whose knowledge existence emanates (i.e., He acts), unlike our life which requires two faculties (perception and action). So His life is identical to His essence. Finally, 'Generous' means He emanates everything without any benefit to Himself; completeness of generosity requires that the recipient actually benefit from the gift (unlike giving to someone who does not need it).

إرادة متجددة
قوة شوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God's knowledge is like human knowledge, derived from objects, or that His attributes are separate parts added to His essence.
Correction: Ghazali insists God's knowledge is creative and not derived; His attributes like Life and Generosity are identical to His essence, not additional faculties.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what kind of knowledge does God possess?
A) Knowledge derived from the forms of things.
B) Knowledge that is invented and causes the existence of its object.
C) A combination of both derived and invented knowledge.
D) Knowledge identical to human knowledge.

Key Takeaway
For Ghazali, divine attributes are not accidental additions to essence; they are the essence itself, understood in relation to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God knows every detail of your life. But does that knowledge feel like it is merely watching, or like it is actively sustaining your very existence each moment? Pause and confess any area where you live as if God is a passive observer rather than the One whose knowledge is constantly creating you anew.
?
You call God 'the Generous' – but do you ever hold back from giving, secretly hoping for a return? God gives without need and without expecting anything back. Where is there a relationship or a possession in your life that you refuse to let go of because you want something in return? Commit to giving something today purely for His sake.
?
If God's life is simple, not split into separate faculties, then every part of your worship is known and witnessed by the same undivided Essence. Are you paying attention to Him with your whole being, or are you distracted, assuming He only 'sees' but doesn't 'hear' or 'will' at the same time? Repent of half-hearted worship and ask for a unified heart.

Identity of God's Attributes with His Essence
Ghazali proves the identity of will, power, knowledge, and essence in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that God's attributes of will, power, and knowledge are identical with His essence, and how this differs from human attributes.

Core Idea
In God, will, power, knowledge, and essence are one—they are not separate.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa tackles a central issue in philosophical theology: the relationship between God's attributes and His essence. Many philosophers and theologians have debated whether God's attributes are additional to His essence or identical with it. Ghazali here presents a powerful argument for identity, drawing on the nature of divine knowledge and contrasting it with human knowledge and action. Let's delve into his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and the translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the initial assertion and the contrast with human knowledge.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail at first.
2
Identify the central claim
Ghazali asserts that will, power, knowledge, and essence are identical in God. Find where he states this and see how he supports it.
The claim is at the beginning: 'فلا تكون الإرادة إلا عين القدرة ...'
3
Understand the two types of knowledge
Ghazali divides knowledge into two kinds: acquired from the object, and invented by the subject. Determine which kind applies to God and why.
God's knowledge is of the second kind; it is creative.
4
Contrast human and divine operation
Examine why in humans, knowledge does not automatically lead to action, but in God it does. This contrast reinforces the identity of attributes in God.
Humans need additional will and physical mechanisms; God is simple and non-composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه مريد ، فلا تكون الإرادة إلا عين القدرة ، ولا القدرة إلا عين العلم ولا العلم إلا عين الذات ، فالكل إذن يرجع إلى عين الذات ، وهذا لأن علمه بالأشياء ، ليس مأخوذاً من الأشياء ، وإلا لكان مستفيداً وصفاً أو كمالا من غيره ، وهو محال في واجب الوجود ، ولكن علمنا على قسمين ، ( علم ) شيء حصل من صورة ذلك الشيء ، كعلمنا بصورة السماء والأرض ، و ( علم ) اخترعناه ، كشيء لم نشاهد صورته ، ولكن صورناه في أنفسنا ، ثم أحدثناه ، فيكون وجود الصورة مستفاداً من العلم ، لا العلم من الوجود ، وعلم الأول بحسب القسم الثانى ، فإن تمثل النظام في ذاته ، سبب لفيضان النظام عن نعم لو كان مجرد حضور صورة نقش أو كتابة خط في نفوسنا ، كافياً في حدوث تلك الصورة ، لكان العلم بعينه منا ، هو القدرة بعينها ، والإرادة بعينها ولكنا لقصورنا ليس يكفى تصورنا ، لإيجاد الصورة ، بل نحتاج ذلك إلى إرادة متجددة ، تنبعث من قوة شوقية ، ليتحرك منهما معاً القوة المحركة للعضل والأعصاب ، فى الأعضاء الآلية ، فيتحرك بحركة العضل والأعصاب ، اليد أو غيرها ، ويتحرك بحركتها ، القلم أو آلة أخرى خارجة ، وتتحرك المادة بحركة القلم ، كالمداد أو غيره ، ثم تحصل الصورة المتصورة في نفوسنا ، فلذلك لم يكن نفس وجود هذه الصورة في نفوسنا ، قدرة ولا إرادة ، بل كانت القدرة فينا عن المبدأ المحرك للعضل ، وهذه الصورة محركة لذلك المحرك للعضل ، الذي هو مبدأ القدرة ، وليس كذلك في واجب الوجود ، فإنه ليس مركباً من أجسام تنبث القوى في أطرافها ، فكانت القدرة ، والإرادة ، والعلم ، والذات ، منه واحداً
He is Willing, so will is only nothing but the very power, and power is only nothing but the very knowledge, and knowledge is only nothing but the very essence, so everything therefore returns to the very essence. And this is because His knowledge of things is not taken from things, otherwise He would be obtaining an attribute or perfection from another, and that is impossible for the Necessary Existence. But our knowledge is of two divisions: (knowledge) something obtained from the form of that thing, like our knowledge of the form of the sky and the earth, and (knowledge) we invent, like something we have not witnessed its form but we form it in ourselves, then we bring it into existence, so the existence of the form comes from knowledge, not knowledge from existence. And His knowledge is according to the second division, for the representation of order in His essence is the cause for the overflowing of order from Him. If the mere presence of an image of an engraving or writing in our souls were sufficient for the occurrence of that image, then knowledge in us would be the very power and the very will. But due to our deficiency, our conception is not sufficient for bringing the image into existence; rather, we need for that a renewed will arising from a desirous force, so that together the moving force of muscles and nerves moves in the instrumental limbs, and by the movement of muscles and nerves the hand or other moves, and by its movement the pen or another external tool moves, and the material moves by the movement of the pen, like ink or other, then the imagined image is obtained in our souls. Therefore, the very existence of this image in our souls is not power nor will; rather, power in us is from the principle that moves the muscle, and this image is a mover of that mover of the muscle, which is the principle of power. And not so in the Necessary Existence, for He is not composed of bodies that emanate forces to their extremities; thus power, will, knowledge, and essence are one from Him.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the identity of God's attributes with His essence. Ghazali begins by asserting that God is 'Mureed' (Willing), so will is nothing other than power, power nothing other than knowledge, and knowledge nothing other than essence; hence all attributes return to the essence. He then explains why: God's knowledge is not derived from things, otherwise He would gain perfection from another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. Human knowledge is of two kinds: knowledge obtained from the form of the thing (like our knowledge of the sky and earth) and knowledge we invent and then bring into existence. In the second kind, the existence of the form comes from knowledge, not the reverse. God's knowledge of the universe corresponds to this second kind—the representation of order in His essence is the cause for the overflowing of order from Him. Ghazali then contrasts human and divine operation: in us, the mere presence of an image in the soul is not sufficient for power or will; we need an additional will and a driving force to move muscles and tools. In God, because He is not composed of bodies with forces distributed to extremities, power, will, knowledge, and essence are one. The key point is that divine attributes are not separate powers but are identical with the simple divine essence.

Key Terms
الإرادة
القدرة
العلم
الذات
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's attributes are separate from His essence, like distinct qualities added to His being.
Correction: Ghazali argues that in God, will, power, knowledge, and essence are identical. They are not separate entities but one simple reality, because God is not composed of parts and His knowledge is creative, not derived.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why are will, power, knowledge, and essence one in God but not in humans?
A) Because God is simple and not composed of bodies with distributed forces.
B) Because God has no need for will.
C) Because human knowledge is always acquired from things.
D) Because God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's core argument: God's simplicity means His attributes are not additional; they are identical with His very essence, unlike human attributes which are distinct due to our composite nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's mercy and His justice, do you ever feel they are pulling in different directions, as if He has conflicting attributes? How would it change your trust in Him to know that in His simple essence, mercy, justice, will, and power are the very same thing—not competing? Will you today confess with your heart that He is one in every sense, even when your mind struggles to see it?
?
Ghazali says our knowledge is often 'taken from things'—we learn by seeing, hearing, experiencing. But God's knowledge is not like that. Are you trying to build your knowledge of God only from what you observe in creation, forgetting that He is the Creator and His knowledge is the source of all things? What would it look like to seek knowledge of Him directly from His revelation instead of relying on your own limited perceptions?
?
If God's will, power, and knowledge are one, then every event in your life is not a result of a divine committee but of a single, simple act of God. Does that simplify your trust in His decree, or does it make it harder? Will you commit today to believing that nothing in your life happens outside that unified will-power-knowledge, and that He is perfectly sufficient in Himself?

The Meaning of God's Name 'Living'
Ghazali defines the name 'Living' for God as Knowing and Active.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's name 'Living' means He is Knowing and Active, and that His life is identical to His essence and unlike human life.

Core Idea
God's life is not like ours; it is His very essence as Knowing and Active.

In this passage, Ghazali clarifies the meaning of one of God's most important names: 'Living' (al-Hayy). He explains that calling God 'Living' does not imply a life like ours, but rather that He is Knowing and Active, and that His life is identical to His essence. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the definitions Ghazali gives.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key claims
Notice that Ghazali equates 'Living' with 'Knowing' and 'Active'. Also note the contrast with human life.
3
Understand the negation
Ghazali says God's life is 'not like our life'. Why does he stress this? Think about anthropomorphism.
This guards against imagining God has a life like a created being.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this definition relates to the concept of divine attributes being identical with essence.
This is a key point in Islamic theology (tawhid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل له : ( حى ) ، لم يرد به إلا أنه عالم علماً ، يفيض عنه الوجود ، الذي فعلا له ، فإن الحى هو الدراك ، فيكون المراد به ذاته مع إضافة إلى الأفعال ، على الوجه الذي ذكرناه . يسمى الفعال لا كحياتنا ، فإنها لا تتم إلا بقوتين مختلفتين ، ينبعث عنهما الإدراك والفعل فحياته عين ذاته أيضاً
And when it is said of Him: 'Living', nothing is meant by it except that He is Knowing with a knowledge from which existence emanates, which is His act, for the Living is the perceiver. So what is meant is His essence with an addition to actions, in the manner we have mentioned. He is called the Active, not like our life, for it is not complete except with two different forces from which perception and action emanate. So His life is also the very essence.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine name 'Living' (al-Hayy). Ghazali states that when we say God is Living, we mean that He is Knowing with a knowledge from which existence flows, which is His act, because 'the Living' is the perceiver. Thus the name indicates His essence together with a relation to action, as previously explained. He is also called 'the Active' (al-Fa''al). This is not like human life, which requires two distinct forces for perception and action. In contrast, God's life is identical to His essence.

Key Terms

دراك
فعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's life is a separate attribute or similar to human life.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that God's life is identical to His essence and not like human life.

Checkpoint

A) He has a life similar to human life.
B) He is Knowing with a knowledge from which existence emanates.
C) He has two different forces for perception and action.
D) He is the cause of all life.

Key Takeaway
God's name 'Living' means He is Knowing and Active, and His life is identical to His essence, not like human life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's life is His knowing and acting, then everything you do is before the One who is always fully present and active. Are there areas of your life where you forget that He is constantly knowing and acting, and act as if He is unaware?
?
Human life is divided between perception and action, often pulling us in different directions. In light of this lesson, does your life reflect a unified purpose for God, or are you torn between competing desires and distractions?
