The True Meaning of God's Generosity
Ghazali defines 'Generous' for God as giving all without need or return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of God's attribute 'Generous' and the two conditions that distinguish divine generosity from human generosity.

Core Idea
True generosity gives all without any self-interest.

When we call God 'Generous', we are making a profound claim about His nature. But what does 'generosity' really mean when applied to God? Ghazali cuts through our everyday assumptions and gives a precise definition that eliminates any hint of need or self-interest. In this passage, he explains two essential conditions for true generosity and shows that only God meets them perfectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning. Let Ghazali's own phrasing set the stage.
2
Identify the two conditions of generosity
The passage lists two conditions for true generosity. Find them and note how each condition eliminates a form of self-interest.
The first condition relates to the recipient, the second to the giver.
3
Compare human and divine generosity
Notice why Ghazali says that every human giver is actually a 'compensator' (musta'id) rather than truly generous. Contrast this with God's generosity.
This highlights the radical difference between creature and Creator.
4
Reflect on the theological payoff
Consider why Ghazali ends by stating that the name 'Generous' indicates God's existence plus action minus purpose, and how that avoids multiplicity in the divine essence.
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل له : جواد، أريد به أنه يفيض عنه الكل . لا لغرض يرجع إليه ، والجود يتم بشيئين : أحدهما ، أن يكون للمنعم عليه فائدة فيما وهب له ، له ، فإن من يهب شيئاً من هو مستغن عنه ، لا يوصف بالجود والثانى أن لا يحتاج الجواد إلى الجود ، فيكون إقدامه على الجود الحاجة نفسه ، وكل من يجود يمدح ويثني عليه ، أو يتخلص من مذمة ، فهو مستعيض ، وليس بجواد، وإنما الجود الحقيقى لله سبحانه وتعالى، فإنه ليس يبغى به خلاصاً عن ذم، ولا كمالاً مستفاداً بمدح ، فيكون الجواد اسماً منبئا عن وجوده مع إضافة إلى الفعل، وسلب للغرض ، فلا يؤدى إلى الكثرة في ذاته .
And when it is said of Him: 'Generous', it is meant that from Him all things emanate, not for a purpose returning to Him. And generosity is completed by two things: one is that the beneficiary should have a benefit in what is given to him, for whoever gives something to one who is needless of it is not described as generous. The second is that the generous one should not need generosity, so that his undertaking of generosity is due to need itself. Everyone who gives is praised or thanked, or frees himself from blame; he is a compensator, not generous. But true generosity belongs to God, the Exalted and Majestic, for He does not seek by it freedom from blame nor a perfection obtained by praise. So 'Generous' is a name indicating His existence with an addition to action and negation of purpose, so it does not lead to multiplicity in His essence.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine name 'Generous' (al-Jawad). Ghazali states that it means that from Him all things emanate, not for a purpose returning to Him. He then gives two conditions for true generosity: first, that the recipient should actually benefit from the gift (giving to someone who is not in need is not generosity); second, that the giver should not be in need of giving (so that giving is not due to his own need). Everyone who gives for praise, thanks, or to avoid blame is actually seeking compensation, not truly generous. True generosity belongs only to God, for He does not seek to free Himself from blame or gain perfection through praise. The name therefore indicates God's existence with an addition to action and a negation of purpose, without implying multiplicity in His essence.

Key Terms
جواد
الجود
الغرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human generosity can be fully analogous to God's generosity.
Correction: Ghazali shows that all human generosity is tainted by self-interest (praise, thanks, avoiding blame). Only God's generosity is absolutely free from any need or purpose, making it the only true generosity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the second condition for true generosity?
A) The giver must be praised.
B) The giver must not need generosity.
C) The gift must be large.
D) The giver must be wealthy.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute 'Generous' means He gives all things to creation without any need or purpose returning to Him, making Him the only truly generous being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of charity or kindness, do you ever catch yourself hoping for recognition or reciprocation? How does Ghazali's definition of true generosity expose the hidden self-interest in your heart? What would it take for you to give purely for God, expecting nothing in return?
?
Consider the times you have served God in prayer, fasting, or charity. Have any of these acts been tainted by a desire to earn status, escape guilt, or secure a reward? If God's generosity is without any purpose, how does your calculation of 'what you get out of it' become a form of compensation? Will you commit to examining your intentions before every act of worship?
?
If only God is truly generous, what does that mean for your relationship with Him? Are you willing to receive His generosity without trying to pay it back, or do you secretly feel you must earn His favor? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in His pure grace and abandon your need to 'deserve' His gifts?

Divine Attributes

Two Interpretations of 'Pure Good'
Ghazali explains 'Pure Good' as existence free from defect or as cause of order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways Ghazali explains the divine attribute 'Pure Good' (Khayr Mahd): as negation of defect and as cause of order.

Core Idea
Pure Good means existence free from defect or the source of all order.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles the meaning of the divine name 'Pure Good'. He offers two complementary interpretations. The first focuses on God's perfection and the non-existence of evil. The second links goodness to being the cause of cosmic order. Understanding these interpretations helps clarify how Islamic theology handles divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the two interpretations signaled by 'famma' and 'wa qad'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Ghazali states the first interpretation (existence free from defect) and the second (cause of order). Note how he explains evil as non-existence.
The first interpretation focuses on negation, the second on positive causality.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on why Ghazali provides two possibilities. How do they avoid attributing a separate positive attribute to God?
Both interpretations treat 'Pure Good' as indicating God's essence with an additional relation or negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : خير محض ، فإما أن يراد به وجوده بريئاً عن النقص وإمكان العدم، فإن الشر لاذات له بل يرجع إلى عدم جوهر ، أو عدم صلاح حال الجوهر ، وإلا فالوجود من حيث إنه وجود – خير ، فيرجع هذا الاسم إلى السلب، لإمكان النقص والشر ، وقد يقال : خير ، لما هو السبب لنظام الأشياء ، والأول مبدأ لنظام كل شيء ، فهو خير ، ويكون الاسم دالا على الوجود ، مع نوع إضافة
And when it is said: 'Pure Good', then either it is meant by it His existence free from defect and possibility of non-existence, for evil has no essence but rather returns to the non-existence of a substance or the non-existence of a suitable state of the substance. Otherwise, existence insofar as it is existence is good, so this name returns to negation of the possibility of defect and evil. And sometimes it is said: 'Good' for that which is the cause of the order of things, and the First is the principle of the order of everything, so He is Good. And the name indicates existence with a kind of addition.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine name 'Pure Good' (Khayr Mahd). Ghazali gives two possible interpretations. The first is that God's existence is free from defect and the possibility of non-existence. He explains that evil has no real essence; it is simply the non-existence of a substance or the non-existence of a proper state of a substance. In itself, existence as such is good. So the name 'Pure Good' primarily indicates negation of defect and evil. The second interpretation is that 'Good' refers to that which is the cause of the order of things; since God is the First Principle of all order, He is Good. In either case, the name indicates God's existence with a kind of addition (i.e., negation of defect or causal relation), not a separate positive attribute.

Key Terms
خير محض
الشر
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that evil is a positive, created entity alongside good.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that evil has no essence (la dhat lahu) but is merely non-existence of a substance or its suitable state. Existence as such is good.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is evil?
A) A positive entity created by God.
B) The non-existence of a substance or its unsuitable state.
C) A separate attribute of God.
D) The opposite of good in equal measure.

Key Takeaway
Pure Good refers either to God's existence free from all defect (negation of evil) or to His being the cause of all order in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil has no real essence but is only the absence of good (non-existence of a substance or its proper state), how do you explain the evil you see or experience in your own life? Does this change how you respond to it, or does it feel like empty theory?
?
Ghazali says existence in itself is good. When you feel your own existence is full of defect or disorder, can you still affirm that your existence—just as existence—is good? What would it cost you to believe that?
?
God is Pure Good as the cause of all order. Look at one area of your life today that feels chaotic or broken. If God is truly the source of order, what step of surrender must you take to align with that order, even if it makes no sense to you?

Meaning of 'Necessary Existence'
Ghazali defines 'Necessary Existence' as existence with no cause and impossibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'Necessary Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud) is defined by the negation of any cause for existence and the impossibility of any cause for non-existence.

Core Idea
Necessity of existence is defined by what it lacks: a cause and the possibility of ceasing.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers by clarifying the term 'Necessary Existence'. Before debating whether the world is necessary or contingent, we must settle what 'necessary' means. This passage gives the precise definition: existence without cause and impossibility of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'with negation of a cause for its existence' and 'impossibility of a cause for its non-existence'.
2
Identify the two negations
Understand that necessity is defined by two negations: no cause for existence, and no possibility of non-existence.
The definition does not describe a positive quality.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Ghazali defines necessity negatively. This sets the foundation for arguing that only God can be necessary.
Contrast with possible existents that have a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : ( واجب الوجود ) ، فمعناه هذا الوجود ، مع سلب علة لوجوده ، وإحالة علة لعدمه أولاً وآخراً
And when it is said: 'Necessary Existence', its meaning is this existence with negation of a cause for its existence and impossibility of a cause for its non-existence, first and last.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the fundamental term 'Necessary Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud). Ghazali states that it means this particular existence (i.e., God's existence) with the negation of any cause for its existence (it is uncaused) and the impossibility of any cause for its non-existence (it cannot cease to be), both initially and perpetually. The definition is purely negative: it denies a cause for existence and denies any possibility of non-existence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
سلب
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'Necessary Existence' is a positive attribute describing God's nature.
Correction: Ghazali's definition is entirely negative: it denies a cause for existence and denies any possibility of non-existence. It does not add a positive predicate.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali define 'Necessary Existence' (Wajib al-Wujud)?
A) Existence with a cause that sustains it.
B) Existence with the negation of a cause for its existence and impossibility of a cause for its non-existence.
C) Existence that depends on another.
D) Existence that is possible.

Key Takeaway
'Necessary Existence' is defined by two negations: no cause for its existence and no possibility of non-existence, both initially and perpetually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence has no cause, what does that mean for your own dependence on Him? Do you live as though you are self-sufficient, or do you consciously acknowledge that every breath is caused by the One who is uncaused?
?
Everything you love—people, health, wealth—can cease to exist. The Necessary Existence cannot. How does that reality reshape what you truly trust in and cling to? Will you let go of something today that you have been holding onto as if it were necessary?
